<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en"><i>The Little Prince</i></h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>

<p><i><b>The Little Prince</b></i> (French: <i><a class="extiw" href="//fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Le_Petit_Prince" title="fr:Le Petit Prince">Le Petit Prince</a></i>; <small>French pronunciation: ​</small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_French" title="Help:IPA for French">[lə.pə.tiˈpʁɛ̃s]</a></span>), first published in 1943, is a <a href="/wiki/Novella" title="Novella">novella</a> and the most famous work of the French aristocrat, writer, poet and pioneering aviator <a href="/wiki/Antoine_de_Saint-Exup%C3%A9ry" title="Antoine de Saint-Exupéry">Antoine de Saint-Exupéry</a> (1900–1944).</p>
<p>The novella is both the most-read and most-translated book in the French language, and was voted the best book of the 20th century in France. Translated into more than 250 languages and dialects (as well as braille),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2005.04.03_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2005.04.03-5"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peak-2011.03_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peak-2011.03-6"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> selling nearly two million copies annually with sales totalling over 140 million copies worldwide,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fast_Company-2014.01.31_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fast_Company-2014.01.31-7"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> it has become <a href="/wiki/List_of_best-selling_books" title="List of best-selling books">one of the best-selling books</a> ever published.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TimesTribune-2012.05.03_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-TimesTribune-2012.05.03-8"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2000.05.09_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2000.05.09-9"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goding-1972_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goding-1972-10"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>Note 3<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After the outbreak of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">Second World War</a> Saint-Exupéry was exiled to North America. In the midst of personal upheavals and failing health, he produced almost half of the writings for which he would be remembered, including a tender tale of loneliness, friendship, love, and loss, in the form of a young alien prince fallen to Earth. An earlier memoir by the author had recounted his aviation experiences in the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara Desert</a>, and he is thought to have drawn on those same experiences in <i>The Little Prince</i>.</p>
<p>Since its first publication in the United States, the novella has been adapted to numerous <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">art forms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Media_(communication)" title="Media (communication)">media</a>, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Audio_recording" title="Audio recording">audio recordings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radio_drama" title="Radio drama">radio plays</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theatre" title="Theatre">live stage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Movie_theater" title="Movie theater">film screen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Television" title="Television">television</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ballet" title="Ballet">ballet</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Opera" title="Opera">operatic</a> works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2005.04.03_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2005.04.03-5"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Statesman-2010.01.14_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Statesman-2010.01.14-13"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>The Little Prince</i> is a poetic tale, with watercolour illustrations by the author, in which a pilot stranded in the desert meets a young prince fallen to Earth from a tiny <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a>. The story is philosophical and includes social criticism, remarking on the strangeness of the adult world. It was written during a period when <a href="/wiki/Antoine_de_Saint-Exup%C3%A9ry" title="Antoine de Saint-Exupéry">Saint-Exupéry</a> fled to North America subsequent to the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_France" title="Battle of France">Fall of France</a> during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>, witnessed first hand by the author and captured in his memoir <a href="/wiki/Flight_to_Arras" title="Flight to Arras">Flight to Arras</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wall_Street_Journal-2014.01.23_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wall_Street_Journal-2014.01.23-14"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> The adult fable, according to one review, is actually "...an allegory of Saint-Exupéry's own life—his search for childhood certainties and interior peace, his mysticism, his belief in human courage and brotherhood.... but also an allusion to the tortured nature of their relationship."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-1993.10.28_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1993.10.28-15"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Though ostensibly styled as a <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_literature" title="Children's literature">children's book</a>, <i>The Little Prince</i> makes several observations about life and human nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_Yorker-2014.04.29_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-New_Yorker-2014.04.29-16"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> For example, Saint-Exupéry tells of a <a href="/wiki/Fox" title="Fox">fox</a> meeting the young prince during his travels on Earth. The story's essence is contained in the lines uttered by the fox to the little prince: <i>On ne voit bien qu'avec le cœur. L'essentiel est invisible pour les yeux.</i> ("One sees clearly only with the heart. What is essential is invisible to the eyes.")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Galembert_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Galembert-17"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> Other key thematic messages are articulated by the fox, such as: <i>Tu deviens responsable pour toujours de ce que tu as apprivoisé.</i> ("You become responsible, forever, for what you have tamed.") and <i>C'est le temps que tu as perdu pour ta rose qui fait ta rose si importante.</i> ("It is the time you have lost for your rose that makes your rose so important.") The fox's messages are arguably the book's most famous quotations because they deal with human relationships.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Plot">Plot</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Plot">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spectacle_des_enfants_du_lyc%C3%A9e.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="510" data-file-width="752" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Spectacle_des_enfants_du_lyc%C3%A9e.jpg/220px-Spectacle_des_enfants_du_lyc%C3%A9e.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Spectacle_des_enfants_du_lyc%C3%A9e.jpg/330px-Spectacle_des_enfants_du_lyc%C3%A9e.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Spectacle_des_enfants_du_lyc%C3%A9e.jpg/440px-Spectacle_des_enfants_du_lyc%C3%A9e.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spectacle_des_enfants_du_lyc%C3%A9e.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Musical_theatre" title="Musical theatre">live stage musical</a> adaptation of <i>The Little Prince</i> at the trilingual <a class="extiw" href="//fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lyc%C3%A9e_Antoine-de-Saint-Exup%C3%A9ry_de_Santiago" title="fr:Lycée Antoine-de-Saint-Exupéry de Santiago">Lycée Antoine de Saint-Exupéry</a> in <a href="/wiki/Santiago" title="Santiago">Santiago</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a> (2011).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The narrator explains that, as a young boy, he once drew a picture of a <a href="/wiki/Boa_constrictor" title="Boa constrictor">boa constrictor</a> with an <a href="/wiki/Elephant" title="Elephant">elephant</a> digesting in its stomach; however, every adult who saw the picture would mistakenly interpret it as a drawing of a hat. Whenever the narrator would try to correct this confusion, he was ultimately advised to set aside drawing and take up a more practical or mature hobby. The narrator laments the lack of creative understanding displayed by adults.</p>
<p>Now an adult himself, the narrator has become a pilot, and, one day, his plane crashes in the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara desert</a>, far from civilization. Here, the narrator is suddenly greeted by a young boy or small man whom he refers to as "the little prince". The little prince asks the narrator to draw a sheep. The narrator first shows him his old picture of the elephant inside the snake, which, to the narrator's surprise, the prince interprets correctly. After a few failed attempts at drawing a good-looking sheep, the narrator simply draws a box in his frustration, claiming that the box holds a sheep inside. Again, to the narrator's surprise, the prince exclaims that this is exactly the picture he wanted. The narrator says that the prince has a strange habit of avoiding directly answering any of the narrator's questions. The prince is described as having golden hair, a scarf, and a lovable laugh.</p>
<p>Over the course of eight days stranded in the desert, as the narrator attempts to repair his plane, the little prince recounts the story of his life. The prince begins by describing life on his tiny home planet: in effect, an <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> the size of a house (which the narrator believes to be the one known as <a href="/wiki/46610_Besixdouze" title="46610 Besixdouze">B-612</a>). The asteroid's most prominent features are three minuscule <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanoes</a> (two active, and one <a href="/wiki/Volcano#volcano_activity" title="Volcano">dormant or extinct</a>) as well as a variety of plants. The prince describes spending his earlier days cleaning the volcanoes and weeding out certain unwanted seeds and sprigs that infest his planet's soil; in particular, pulling out <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baobab" title="Baobab">baobab</a> trees that are constantly trying to grow and overrun the surface. The prince appears to want a sheep to eat such undesirable plants, until the narrator informs him that a sheep will even eat roses with thorns. Upon hearing this, the prince tells of his love for a mysterious rose that suddenly began growing on the asteroid's surface some time ago. The prince says he nourished the rose and listened to her when she told him to make a screen or glass globe to protect her from the cold wind. Although the prince fell in love with the rose, he also began to feel that she was taking advantage of him, and he resolved to leave the planet to explore the rest of the universe. Although the rose finally apologized for her <a href="/wiki/Vanity" title="Vanity">vanity</a>, and the two reconciled, she encouraged him to go ahead with his journey and so he traveled onward.</p>
<p>The prince has since visited six other asteroids, each of which was inhabited by a foolish, narrow-minded adult, including: a king with no subjects; a conceited man, who believed himself the most admirable person on his otherwise uninhabited planet; a drunkard who drank to forget the shame of being a drunkard; a businessman who endlessly counted the stars and absurdly claimed to own them all; a lamplighter who mindlessly extinguished and relighted a lamp every single minute; and an elderly <a href="/wiki/Geographer" title="Geographer">geographer</a>, so wrapped up in theory that he never actually explored the world that he claimed to be mapping. When the geographer asked the prince to describe his home, the prince mentioned the rose, and the geographer explained that he does not record "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ephemeral" title="Ephemeral">ephemeral</a>" things, such as roses. The prince was shocked and hurt by this revelation, since the rose was of great importance to him on a personal level. The geographer recommended that the prince next visit the planet <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Little_Prince_(theatre_adaptation).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1406" data-file-width="2114" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/The_Little_Prince_%28theatre_adaptation%29.jpg/220px-The_Little_Prince_%28theatre_adaptation%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/The_Little_Prince_%28theatre_adaptation%29.jpg/330px-The_Little_Prince_%28theatre_adaptation%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/The_Little_Prince_%28theatre_adaptation%29.jpg/440px-The_Little_Prince_%28theatre_adaptation%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Little_Prince_(theatre_adaptation).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
One of <a href="/wiki/List_of_The_Little_Prince_adaptations" title="List of The Little Prince adaptations">numerous stage adaptations</a> of Saint-Exupéry's child and adult fable, this one at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Minnesota" title="University of Minnesota">University of Minnesota</a>'s Rarig Center Proscenium (2010).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On Earth, the prince landed in the desert, leading him to believe that Earth was uninhabited. He then met a yellow snake that claimed to have the power to return him to his home, if he ever wished to return. The prince next met a desert flower, who told him that she had only seen a handful of men in this part of the world and that they had no roots, letting the wind blow them around and living hard lives. After climbing the highest mountain he had ever seen, the prince hoped to see the whole of Earth, thus finding the people; however, he saw only the enormous, desolate landscape. When the prince called out, his echo answered him, which he interpreted as the mocking voices of others. Eventually, the prince encountered a whole row of rosebushes, becoming downcast at having once thought that his own rose was unique. He began to feel that he was not a great prince at all, as his planet contained only three tiny volcanoes and a flower that he now thought of as common. He lay down in the grass and wept, until a <a href="/wiki/Fennec_fox" title="Fennec fox">fox</a> came along. The fox desired to be tamed and explained to the prince that his rose really was indeed unique and special, because she was the object of the prince's love. The fox also explained that, in a way, the prince had tamed the rose, and that this is why the prince was now feeling so responsible for her. The prince then took time to tame the fox, though the two ultimately parted ways, teary-eyed. The prince next came across a railway switchman, who told him how passengers constantly rushed from one place to another aboard trains, never satisfied with where they were and not knowing what they were after; only the children among them ever bothered to look out the windows. A merchant then talked to the prince about his product, a pill that eliminated thirst, which was very popular, saving people fifty-three minutes a week. The prince replied that he would instead gladly use that extra time to go around finding fresh water.</p>
<p>Back in the present moment, it is the eighth day after the narrator's plane-crash and the narrator is dying of thirst; fortunately, he and the prince together find a well. The narrator later finds the prince talking to the snake, discussing his return home and eager to see his rose again, who he worries has been left to fend for herself. The prince bids an emotional farewell to the narrator and states that if it looks as though he has died, it is only because his body was too heavy to take with him to his planet. The prince warns the narrator not to watch him leave, as it will make him upset. The narrator, realizing what will happen, refuses to leave the prince's side; the prince consoles the narrator by saying that he only need look at the stars to think of the prince's lovable laughter, and that it will seem as if all the stars are laughing. The prince then walks away from the narrator and allows the snake to bite him, falling without making a sound.</p>
<p>The next morning, the narrator tries to look for the prince, but is unable to find his body. The story ends with the narrator's drawing of the landscape where the prince and the narrator met and where the snake took the prince's life. The narrator requests that anyone in that area encountering a small man who refuses to answer questions should contact the narrator immediately.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tone_and_writing_style">Tone and writing style</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Tone and writing style">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The story of <i>The Little Prince</i> is recalled in a sombre, measured tone by the pilot-narrator, in memory of his small friend, "a memorial to the prince—not just to the prince, but also to the time the prince and the narrator had together".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shmoop.a_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shmoop.a-18"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>The Little Prince</i> was created when <a href="/wiki/Antoine_de_Saint-Exup%C3%A9ry" title="Antoine de Saint-Exupéry">Saint-Exupéry</a> was "...an expatriate and distraught about what was going on in his country and in the world."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fast_Company-2014.01.31_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fast_Company-2014.01.31-7"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> It was written during his 27 month sojourn in North America, almost as a sort of <a href="/wiki/Credo" title="Credo">credo</a>, "carefully employing the expressions of despair, loneliness, and triumph throughout its plotline."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daily_Mail-2014.02.11_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Daily_Mail-2014.02.11-19"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>According to one analysis, "the story of the Little Prince features a lot of fantastical, unrealistic elements... You can't ride a flock of birds to another planet... The fantasy of the Little Prince works because the logic of the story is based on the imagination of children, rather than the strict realism of adults."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Danuscript.a_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Danuscript.a-20"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>An exquisite literary perfectionist akin to the 19th century French poet <a href="/wiki/St%C3%A9phane_Mallarm%C3%A9" title="Stéphane Mallarmé">Stéphane Mallarmé</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-1955.07.10_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1955.07.10-21"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> Saint-Exupéry produced draft pages "covered with fine lines of handwriting, much of it painstakingly crossed out, with one word left standing where there were a hundred words, one sentence substitut[ing] for a page...."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atlantic_Monthly-1947.04_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Atlantic_Monthly-1947.04-22"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> He worked "long hours with great concentration". According to the author himself it was extremely difficult to start his creative writing processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-1941.01.19_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1941.01.19-23"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> Biographer Paul Webster wrote of the aviator-author's style "Behind Saint-Exupéry's quest for perfection was a laborious process of editing and rewriting which reduced original drafts by as much as two-thirds of their length."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-1993.10.28_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1993.10.28-15"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> The French author frequently wrote at night, usually starting about 11 p.m. accompanied by a tray of strong black coffee. In 1942 Saint-Exupéry related to his American English teacher, Adèle Breaux, that at such a time of night he felt "free" and able to concentrate, "writing for hours without feeling tired or sleepy" until he instantaneously dozed off.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atlantic_Monthly-1947.04_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Atlantic_Monthly-1947.04-22"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> He would wake up later, in daylight, still at his desk with his head on his arms. Saint-Exupéry stated it was the only way he could work, as once he started a writing project it became an obsession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>Note 4<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>Note 5<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Inspirations">Inspirations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Inspirations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Events_and_characters">Events and characters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Events and characters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sahara_Crash_-1935-_copyright_free_in_Egypt_3634_StEx_1_-cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="363" data-file-width="276" height="289" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Sahara_Crash_-1935-_copyright_free_in_Egypt_3634_StEx_1_-cropped.jpg/220px-Sahara_Crash_-1935-_copyright_free_in_Egypt_3634_StEx_1_-cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/89/Sahara_Crash_-1935-_copyright_free_in_Egypt_3634_StEx_1_-cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/89/Sahara_Crash_-1935-_copyright_free_in_Egypt_3634_StEx_1_-cropped.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sahara_Crash_-1935-_copyright_free_in_Egypt_3634_StEx_1_-cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Saint-Exupéry next to his crashed Simoun (lacking an all-critical radio) after crashing into the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara Desert</a> about 3 am during an air race to <a href="/wiki/Ho_Chi_Minh_City" title="Ho Chi Minh City">Saigon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>. His survival ordeal was about to begin (Egypt, 1935).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <i>The Little Prince</i>, its narrator, the pilot, talks of being stranded in the desert beside his crashed aircraft. This account clearly drew on <a href="/wiki/Antoine_de_Saint-Exup%C3%A9ry" title="Antoine de Saint-Exupéry">Antoine de Saint-Exupéry</a>'s own experience in the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a>, an ordeal described in detail in his 1939 memoir <i><a href="/wiki/Wind,_Sand_and_Stars" title="Wind, Sand and Stars">Wind, Sand and Stars</a></i> (original French: <i><a class="extiw" href="//fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terre_des_hommes" title="fr:Terre des hommes">Terre des hommes</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_Yorker-2014.04.29_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-New_Yorker-2014.04.29-16"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On December 30, 1935, at 02:45 am, after 19 hours and 44 minutes in the air, Saint-Exupéry, along with his copilot-navigator André Prévot, crashed in the Sahara desert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchiff1996258_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchiff1996258-30"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> They were attempting to break the speed record for a Paris-to-Saigon flight in a then-popular type of air race, called a <i>raid</i>, and win a prize of 150,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Francs" title="Francs">francs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schiff-1996a_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schiff-1996a-31"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> Their plane was a <a href="/wiki/Caudron" title="Caudron">Caudron</a> <a href="/wiki/Caudron_Simoun" title="Caudron Simoun">C-630 Simoun</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>Note 6<span>]</span></a></sup> and the crash site is thought to have been near to the <a href="/wiki/Wadi_El_Natrun" title="Wadi El Natrun">Wadi Natrun</a> valley, close to the <a href="/wiki/Nile_Delta" title="Nile Delta">Nile Delta</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchiff1996263_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchiff1996263-33"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Both miraculously survived the crash, only to face rapid dehydration in the intense desert heat. Their maps were primitive and ambiguous. Lost among the sand dunes with a few grapes, a thermos of coffee, a single orange, and some wine, the pair had only one day's worth of liquid. They both began to see <a href="/wiki/Mirage" title="Mirage">mirages</a>, which were quickly followed by more vivid <a href="/wiki/Hallucination" title="Hallucination">hallucinations</a>. By the second and third days, they were so dehydrated that they stopped sweating altogether. Finally, on the fourth day, a <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">Bedouin</a> on a camel discovered them and administered a native rehydration treatment that saved Saint-Exupéry and Prévot's lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schiff-1996a_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schiff-1996a-31"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The prince's home, "Asteroid B-612", was likely derived as a progression of one of the planes Saint-Exupéry flew as an <a href="/wiki/Mail" title="Mail">airmail pilot</a>, which bore the serial number "A-612". During his service as a mail pilot in the North African Sahara desert, Saint-Exupéry had viewed a <a href="/wiki/Fennec_fox" title="Fennec fox">fennec</a> (desert sand fox), which most likely inspired him to create the fox character in the book. In a letter written to his sister Didi from the Western Sahara's <a href="/wiki/Cape_Juby" title="Cape Juby">Cape Juby</a>, where he was the manager of an airmail stopover station in 1928, he tells of raising a fennec which he adored.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In the novella the Wise Fox, believed to be modelled after the author's intimate New York City friend Silvia Hamilton Reinhardt, tells the prince that his rose is unique and special, because she is the one that he loves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-1993.09.19_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1993.09.19-34"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> The novella's iconic phrase, "One sees clearly only with the heart", is believed to have been suggested by Silvia Hamilton.</p>
<p>The fearsome, grasping <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baobab" title="Baobab">baobab trees</a>, researchers have contended, were meant to represent <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazism</a> attempting to destroy the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-1993.09.19_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1993.09.19-34"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> The little prince's reassurance to the pilot that his dying body is only an empty shell resembles the last words of Antoine's dying younger brother François, who told the author, from his deathbed: "Don't worry. I'm all right. I can't help it. It's my body".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AirmansOdyssey_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-AirmansOdyssey-35"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The literary device of presenting philosophical and social commentaries in the form of the impressions gained by a fictional extraterrestrial visitor to Earth had already been used by the philosopher and satirist <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a> in his story <i><a href="/wiki/Microm%C3%A9gas" title="Micromégas">Micromégas</a></i> of 1752 — a classic work of French literature with which Saint-Exupéry was likely familiar.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Rose">The Rose</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: The Rose">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Consuelo_en_1942_%C3%A0_Montr%C3%A9al.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="554" data-file-width="420" height="264" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Consuelo_en_1942_%C3%A0_Montr%C3%A9al.jpg/200px-Consuelo_en_1942_%C3%A0_Montr%C3%A9al.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Consuelo_en_1942_%C3%A0_Montr%C3%A9al.jpg/300px-Consuelo_en_1942_%C3%A0_Montr%C3%A9al.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Consuelo_en_1942_%C3%A0_Montr%C3%A9al.jpg/400px-Consuelo_en_1942_%C3%A0_Montr%C3%A9al.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Consuelo_en_1942_%C3%A0_Montr%C3%A9al.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Rose in <i>The Little Prince</i> was likely inspired by Saint-Exupéry's Salvadoran wife, Consuelo (Montreal, 1942).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Many researchers believe that the prince's petulant, vain rose was inspired by Saint-Exupéry's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salvadoran" title="Salvadoran">Salvadoran</a> wife <a href="/wiki/Consuelo_de_Saint_Exup%C3%A9ry" title="Consuelo de Saint Exupéry">Consuelo</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-1993.09.19_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1993.09.19-34"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWebster1993248.E2.80.93251_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWebster1993248.E2.80.93251-36"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> with the small home planet being inspired by her small home country <a href="/wiki/El_Salvador" title="El Salvador">El Salvador</a>, also known as "The Land of Volcanoes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaint_Exup.C3.A9ry.2C_Consuelo_de2003_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaint_Exup.C3.A9ry.2C_Consuelo_de2003-37"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite a raucous marriage, Saint-Exupéry kept Consuelo close to his heart and portrayed her as the prince's Rose whom he tenderly protects with a wind screen and under a glass dome on his tiny planet. Saint-Exupéry's infidelity and the doubts of his marriage are symbolized by the vast field of roses the prince encounters during his visit to Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_Yorker-2014.04.29_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-New_Yorker-2014.04.29-16"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>This view of Consuelo was described by biographer Paul Webster who stated she was "the muse to whom Saint-Exupéry poured out his soul in copious letters..... Consuelo was the rose in <i>The Little Prince.</i> "I should have judged her by her acts and not by her words," says the prince. "She wrapped herself around me and enlightened me. I should never have fled. I should have guessed at the tenderness behind her poor ruses."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-1993.10.28_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1993.10.28-15"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Prince">The Prince</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: The Prince">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Antoine_de_Saint-Exup%C3%A9ry" title="Antoine de Saint-Exupéry">Saint-Exupéry</a> may have drawn inspiration for the prince's character and appearance from his own self as a youth, as during his early years friends and family called him <i>le Roi-Soleil</i> (the Sun King) due to his golden curly hair. The author had also met a precocious eight-year-old with curly blond hair while residing with a family in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quebec_City,_Canada" title="Quebec City, Canada">Quebec City, Canada</a> in 1942, <a class="extiw" href="//fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_De_Koninck" title="fr:Thomas De Koninck">Thomas De Koninck</a>, the son of philosopher <a href="/wiki/Charles_De_Koninck" title="Charles De Koninck">Charles De Koninck</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchiff1996378_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchiff1996378-38"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown2004_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown2004-39"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-La_Presse-2013.09.13_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-La_Presse-2013.09.13-40"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> Another possible inspiration for the little prince has been suggested as Land Morrow Lindbergh, the young, golden-haired son of the pioneering American aviator <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lindbergh" title="Charles Lindbergh">Charles Lindbergh</a> and his wife <a href="/wiki/Anne_Morrow_Lindbergh" title="Anne Morrow Lindbergh">Anne Morrow</a> all of whom met him during an overnight stay at their <a href="/wiki/Long_Island" title="Long Island">Long Island</a> home in 1939.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunning1989_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunning1989-41"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffman_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffman-42"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>Note 7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Some have seen the prince as a Christ figure, as the child is sin-free and "believes in a life after death", subsequently returning to his personal heaven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BestNotes.com-a_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-BestNotes.com-a-44"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Life_(magazine)" title="Life (magazine)"><i>Life</i></a> photojournalist <a href="/wiki/John_Phillips_(photographer)" title="John Phillips (photographer)">John Phillips</a> provided a direct answer to the question of the character's origin when he questioned the author-aviator on his inspiration for the child character. After Phillips posed the question, Saint-Exupéry replied that "...one day he looked down on what he thought was a blank sheet and saw a small childlike figure." When Phillips asked who the figure was, the author replied "I'm the Little Prince".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrainPickings_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-BrainPickings-45"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>One of Saint-Exupéry's earliest literary references to a small prince is to be found in his second news dispatch from <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a>, dated May 14, 1935. In his writings as a special correspondent for <i><a href="/wiki/Paris-Soir" title="Paris-Soir">Paris-Soir</a></i> the author described his transit from France to the U.S.S.R. by train. Late at night during the train trip he ventured from his first class accommodation into the third class carriages, where he came upon large groups of Polish families huddled together, returning to their homeland. His commentary not only described a diminutive prince, but also touched on several other themes Saint-Exupéry incorporated into various philosophical writings:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saint-Exup.C3.A9ry-Sense.of.Life_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saint-Exup.C3.A9ry-Sense.of.Life-46"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<table class="cquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
<tr>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">“</td>
<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;"><tt>I sat down [facing a sleeping] couple. Between the man and the woman a child had hollowed himself out a place and fallen asleep. He turned in his slumber, and in the dim lamplight I saw his face. What an adorable face! A golden fruit had been born of these two peasants..... This is a musician's face, I told myself. This is the child Mozart. This is a life full of beautiful promise. Little princes in legends are not different from this. Protected, sheltered, cultivated, what could not this child become? When by mutation a new rose is born in a garden, all gardeners rejoice. They isolate the rose, tend it, foster it. But there is no gardener for men. This little Mozart will be shaped like the rest by the common stamping machine.... This little Mozart is condemned.</tt></td>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">”</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="cquotecite" colspan="3" style="border: none; padding-right: 4%">
<p style="font-size: smaller; text-align: right;"><cite style="font-style: normal;">—A Sense of Life: En Route to the U.S.S.R.</cite></p>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antoine_de_Saint-Exup%C3%A9ry.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="326" data-file-width="450" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Antoine_de_Saint-Exup%C3%A9ry.jpg/220px-Antoine_de_Saint-Exup%C3%A9ry.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Antoine_de_Saint-Exup%C3%A9ry.jpg/330px-Antoine_de_Saint-Exup%C3%A9ry.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Antoine_de_Saint-Exup%C3%A9ry.jpg/440px-Antoine_de_Saint-Exup%C3%A9ry.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antoine_de_Saint-Exup%C3%A9ry.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The writer-aviator on <i><a href="/wiki/Lake_Saint-Louis" title="Lake Saint-Louis">Lac Saint-Louis</a></i> during a speaking tour in support of France after its armistice with Germany. He started his work on the novella shortly after returning to the United States (Quebec,1942).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Upon the outbreak of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antoine_de_Saint-Exup%C3%A9ry" title="Antoine de Saint-Exupéry">Saint-Exupéry</a>, a laureate of several of France's highest literary awards and a successful pioneering <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aviator" title="Aviator">aviator</a> prior to the war, initially flew with a reconnaissance squadron as a reserve military pilot in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arm%C3%A9e_de_l%27Air" title="Armée de l'Air">Armée de l'Air</a></i> (French Air Force).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_Yorker-2014.04.29_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-New_Yorker-2014.04.29-16"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> After France's defeat in 1940 and its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armistice_with_France_(Second_Compi%C3%A8gne)" title="Armistice with France (Second Compiègne)">armistice with Germany</a>, he and <a href="/wiki/Consuelo_de_Saint_Exup%C3%A9ry" title="Consuelo de Saint Exupéry">his wife Consuelo</a> fled occupied France and sojourned in North America, with Saint-Exupéry first arriving by himself at the very end of December 1940. His intention for the visit was to convince the United States to quickly enter the war against <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Axis_powers" title="Axis powers">Axis forces</a>, and he soon became one of the expatriate voices of the <a href="/wiki/French_Resistance" title="French Resistance">French Resistance</a>. In the midst of personal upheavals and failing health he produced almost half of the writings he would be remembered for, including a tender tale of loneliness, friendship, love and loss, in the form of a young prince fallen to Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-1993.05.30_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1993.05.30-47"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> An earlier memoir by the author recounted his aviation experiences in the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> and he is thought to have drawn on those same experiences for use as plot elements in <i>The Little Prince</i>.</p>
<p>Saint-Exupéry wrote and illustrated the manuscript during the summer and fall of 1942. Although greeted warmly by French-speaking Americans and by fellow expatriates who had preceded him to New York, his 27 month stay would be marred by health problems and racked with periods of severe stress, martial and marital strife. These included partisan attacks on the author's neutral stance towards ardent <a href="/wiki/Gaullism" title="Gaullism">French Gaullist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vichy_France" title="Vichy France">collaborationist Vichy</a> supporters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> According to Saint-Exupéry's American translator (the author was unable to become proficient in English), "[h]e was restless and unhappy in exile, seeing no way to fight again for his country and refusing to take part in the political quarrels that set Frenchman against Frenchman".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atlantic_Monthly-1947.04_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-Atlantic_Monthly-1947.04-22"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> However the period was to be both a "dark but productive time" during which he created three important works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wall_Street_Journal-2014.01.23_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wall_Street_Journal-2014.01.23-14"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Between January 1941 and April 1943 the Saint-Exupérys lived in two penthouse apartments on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_Park_South" title="Central Park South">Central Park South</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-1989-05-12_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1989-05-12-49"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> then the <a href="/wiki/Delamater-Bevin_Mansion" title="Delamater-Bevin Mansion">Bevin House</a> mansion in <a href="/wiki/Asharoken,_New_York" title="Asharoken, New York">Asharoken, Long Island, N.Y.</a>, and still later at a rented house on Beekman Place in New York City.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchiff1996380_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchiff1996380-50"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2000.09.10_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2000.09.10-51"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> The couple also stayed in <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, for five weeks during the late spring of 1942, where they met a precocious eight-year-old boy with blond curly hair, Thomas, the son of philosopher <a href="/wiki/Charles_De_Koninck" title="Charles De Koninck">Charles De Koninck</a> with whom the Saint-Exupéry's resided.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schiff-2006_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schiff-2006-52"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hannibal_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hannibal-53"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chesterton_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chesterton-54"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-QuebecVille_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-QuebecVille-55"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> During an earlier visit to Long Island in August 1939, Saint-Exupéry had also met Land Morrow Lindbergh, the young, golden-haired son of the pioneering American aviator <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lindbergh" title="Charles Lindbergh">Charles Lindbergh</a> and his wife <a href="/wiki/Anne_Morrow_Lindbergh" title="Anne Morrow Lindbergh">Anne Morrow</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunning1989_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunning1989-41"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffman_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffman-42"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After returning to the United States from his Quebec speaking tour, Saint-Exupéry was pressed to work on a children's book by Elizabeth Reynal, one of the wives of his U.S. publisher, <a href="/wiki/Reynal_%26_Hitchcock" title="Reynal &amp; Hitchcock">Reynal &amp; Hitchcock</a>. The French wife of Eugene Reynal had closely observed Saint-Exupéry for several months, and noting his ill health and high stress levels, suggested to him that working on a children's story would help.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchiff1996278_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchiff1996278-56"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>Note 8<span>]</span></a></sup> The author wrote and illustrated <i>The Little Prince</i> at various locations in New York City, but principally in the Long Island north-shore community of <a href="/wiki/Asharoken,_New_York" title="Asharoken, New York">Asharoken</a> in mid-to-late 1942, with the manuscript being completed in October.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2000.09.10_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2000.09.10-51"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schiff-2006_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schiff-2006-52"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schiff-2006b_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schiff-2006b-59"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BevinFront.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="400" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/BevinFront.jpg/220px-BevinFront.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/BevinFront.jpg/330px-BevinFront.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0a/BevinFront.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BevinFront.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bevin_House" title="Bevin House">Bevin House</a> on <a href="/wiki/Long_Island" title="Long Island">Long Island, N.Y.</a>, one of the locations where <i>The Little Prince</i> was written during the summer and fall of 1942.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2000.09.10_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2000.09.10-51"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although the book was started in his <a href="/wiki/59th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="59th Street (Manhattan)">Central Park South</a> penthouse, Saint-Exupéry soon found New York City's noise and sweltering summer heat too uncomfortable to work in, so Consuelo was dispatched to find improved accommodations. After spending some time at an unsuitable clapboard country house in <a href="/wiki/Westport,_Connecticut" title="Westport, Connecticut">Westport, Connecticut</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-1942.12.06_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1942.12.06-57"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> the newer result was to be the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bevin_House" title="Bevin House">Bevin House</a>, a 22 room mansion in <a href="/wiki/Asharoken,_New_York" title="Asharoken, New York">Asharoken</a> overlooking <a href="/wiki/Long_Island_Sound" title="Long Island Sound">Long Island Sound</a>. The author-aviator initially complained, "I wanted a hut [but it's] the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Versailles" title="Palace of Versailles">Palace of Versailles</a>"; but as the weeks wore on and the author became invested in his project, the home would become "....a haven for writing, the best place I have ever had anywhere in my life". He devoted himself to the book on mostly midnight shifts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atlantic_Monthly-1947.04_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-Atlantic_Monthly-1947.04-22"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> usually starting at about 11 p.m., fueled by helpings of scrambled eggs on English muffins, gin and tonics, Coca-Colas, cigarettes and numerous reviews by friends and expatriates who dropped in to see their famous countryman. Included among the reviewers was Consuelo's Swiss writer paramour <a href="/wiki/Denis_de_Rougemont" title="Denis de Rougemont">Denis de Rougemont</a>, who also modeled for a painting of the Little Prince lying on his stomach, feet and arms extended up in the air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-1993.05.30_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1993.05.30-47"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2000.09.10_51-3"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2000.09.10-51"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> De Rougemont would later help Consuelo write her autobiography, <i>The Tale of the Rose</i>, as well as write his own biography of Saint-Exupéry.</p>
<p>While the author's personal life was frequently chaotic, his creative process while writing was disciplined. Christine Nelson, curator of literary and historical manuscripts at the <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Library_%26_Museum" title="Morgan Library &amp; Museum">Morgan Library and Museum</a> which had obtained Saint-Exupéry's original manuscript in 1968 stated: "On the one hand, he had a clear vision for the shape, tone, and message of the story. On the other hand, he was ruthless about chopping out entire passages that just weren't quite right", eventually distilling the 30,000 word manuscript, accompanied by small illustrations and sketches, to approximately half its original length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fast_Company_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fast_Company-60"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> The story, the curator added, was created when he was "...an ex-patriate and distraught about what was going on in his country and in the world."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fast_Company-2014.01.31_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Fast_Company-2014.01.31-7"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The large white <a href="/wiki/Second_French_Empire" title="Second French Empire">Second French Empire</a> style mansion, hidden behind tall trees, afforded the writer a multitude of work environments although he usually wrote at a large dining table. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atlantic_Monthly-1947.04_22-4"><a href="#cite_note-Atlantic_Monthly-1947.04-22"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> It also allowed him to alternately work on his writings, and then on his sketches and watercolours for hours at a time, moving his armchair and paint easel from the library towards the parlor one room at a time in order to follow the sun's light. His meditative view of the sunsets at the Bevin House eventually became part of the gist of <i>The Little Prince</i>, in which 43 daily sunsets would be discussed. "On your planet..." the story told, "...all you need do is move your chair a few steps."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-1993.05.30_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1993.05.30-47"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2000.09.10_51-4"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2000.09.10-51"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>Note 9<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Manuscript">Manuscript</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Manuscript">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The original 140-page <a href="/wiki/Manuscript" title="Manuscript">autograph manuscript</a> of <i>The Little Prince</i>, along with various drafts and trial drawings, were acquired from the author's close friend Silvia Hamilton in 1968 by curator Herbert Cahoon of the Pierpont Morgan Library (now <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Morgan_Library_%26_Museum" title="The Morgan Library &amp; Museum">The Morgan Library &amp; Museum</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a>, New York City.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2000.05.09_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2000.05.09-9"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ThreeTomatoes_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-ThreeTomatoes-62"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2000.05.17_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2000.05.17-63"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> It is the only known surviving handwritten draft of the complete work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daily_Mail-2014.02.11_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Daily_Mail-2014.02.11-19"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fine_Books_Magazine-2013.12.03_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fine_Books_Magazine-2013.12.03-64"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> The manuscript's pages include large amounts of the author's prose that was <a href="/wiki/Strikethrough" title="Strikethrough">struck-through</a> and therefore not published as part of the first edition. In addition to the manuscript, several <a href="/wiki/Watercolor_painting" title="Watercolor painting">watercolour illustrations</a> by the author are also held by the museum. They were not part of the first edition. The institution has marked both the 50th and 70th anniversaries of the novella's publication, along with the a centenary celebration of the author's birth, with major exhibitions of <a href="/wiki/Antoine_de_Saint-Exup%C3%A9ry" title="Antoine de Saint-Exupéry">Antoine de Saint-Exupéry</a>'s literary works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-1993.09.19_34-3"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1993.09.19-34"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2014.01.23.a_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2014.01.23.a-65"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> Physically, the manuscript's <a href="/wiki/Onionskin" title="Onionskin">onion skin media</a> has become brittle and subject to damage. Saint-Exupéry's handwriting is described as being doctor-like, verging on indecipherable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-La_Presse-2014.01.27_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-La_Presse-2014.01.27-66"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The story's keynote <a href="/wiki/Aphorism" title="Aphorism">aphorism</a>, <i>On ne voit bien qu'avec le cœur. L'essentiel est invisible pour les yeux</i> ("One sees clearly only with the heart. What is essential is invisible to the eye") was reworded and rewritten some 15 times before achieving its final phrasing. Saint-Exupéry also used his newly purchased $700 <a href="/wiki/Dictaphone" title="Dictaphone">Dictaphone</a> recorder to produce oral drafts for his typist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atlantic_Monthly-1947.04_22-5"><a href="#cite_note-Atlantic_Monthly-1947.04-22"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ThreeTomatoes_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-ThreeTomatoes-62"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> His initial 30,000 word working manuscript was distilled to less than half its original size through laborious editing sessions. Multiple versions of its many pages were created and its prose then polished over several drafts, with the author occasionally telephoning friends at 2:00 a.m. to solicit opinions on his newly written passages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atlantic_Monthly-1947.04_22-6"><a href="#cite_note-Atlantic_Monthly-1947.04-22"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Many pages and illustrations were cut from the finished work as he sought to maintain a sense of ambiguity to the story's theme and messages. Included among the deletions in its 17th chapter were references to locales in New York, such as the <a href="/wiki/Rockefeller_Center" title="Rockefeller Center">Rockefeller Center</a> and <a href="/wiki/Long_Island" title="Long Island">Long Island</a>. Other deleted pages described the prince's vegetarian diet and the garden on his home asteroid that included beans, radishes, potatoes and tomatoes, but which lacked fruit trees that might have overwhelmed the prince's planetoid. Deleted chapters discussed visits to other asteroids occupied by a retailer brimming with marketing phrases, and an inventor whose creation could produce any object desired at a touch of its controls. Likely the result of the ongoing war in Europe weighing on Saint-Exupéry's shoulders, the author produced a sombre three page <a href="/wiki/Epilogue" title="Epilogue">epilogue</a> lamenting "On one star someone has lost a friend, on another someone is ill, on another someone is at war...", with the story's pilot-narrator noting of The Prince: "he sees all that. . . . For him, the night is hopeless. And for me, his friend, the night is also hopeless." The draft epilogue was also omitted from the novella's printing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ThreeTomatoes_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-ThreeTomatoes-62"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In April 2012 a Parisian auction house announced the discovery of two previously unknown draft manuscript pages that had been found and that included new text.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TimesTribune-2012.05.03_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-TimesTribune-2012.05.03-8"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guardian-2012.05.04_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-2012.05.04-67"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> In the newly discovered material the Prince meets his first Earthling after his arrival. The person he meets is an "ambassador of the human spirit".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TimesTribune-2012.05.03_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-TimesTribune-2012.05.03-8"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guardian-2012.05.04_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-2012.05.04-67"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> The ambassador is too busy to talk, saying he is searching for a missing six letter word: "I am looking for a six-letter word that starts with G that means 'gargling'," he says. Saint-Exupéry's text does not say what the word is, but experts believe it could be "guerre" (or "war"). The novella thus takes a more politicized tack with an anti-war sentiment, as 'to gargle' in French is an informal reference to 'honour', which the author may have viewed as a key factor in military confrontations between nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guardian-2012.05.04_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-2012.05.04-67"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AssociatedPress-2012.05.03_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-AssociatedPress-2012.05.03-68"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd><i>Further information: <a href="/wiki/The_Little_Prince#Morgan_exhibitions" title="The Little Prince">Morgan exhibitions</a></i></dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dedication">Dedication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Dedication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Saint-Exupéry met <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9on_Werth" title="Léon Werth">Léon Werth</a> (1878–1955), a writer and art critic, in 1931. Werth soon became Saint-Exupery's closest friend outside of his <a href="/wiki/A%C3%A9ropostale_(aviation)" title="Aéropostale (aviation)">Aeropostale</a> associates. Werth was an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anarchist" title="Anarchist">anarchist</a>, a leftist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bolshevik" title="Bolshevik">Bolshevik</a> supporter and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jew" title="Jew">of Jewish descent</a>, twenty-two years older than Saint-Exupéry. Werth was Saint-Exupéry's very opposite.</p>
<p>Saint-Exupéry dedicated two books to him, <i><a class="extiw" href="//fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lettre_%C3%A0_un_otage" title="fr:Lettre à un otage">Lettre à un otage</a></i> (<i>Letter to a Hostage</i>) and <i>Le Petit Prince</i> (<i>The Little Prince</i>), and referred to Werth in three more of his works. At the beginning of the Second World War while writing <i>The Little Prince</i>, Saint-Exupéry lived in his downtown New York City apartment, thinking of his native France and his friends. Werth spent the war unobtrusively in <a href="/wiki/Saint-Amour,_Jura" title="Saint-Amour, Jura">Saint-Amour</a>, his village in the <a href="/wiki/Jura_(department)" title="Jura (department)">Jura</a>, a mountainous region near Switzerland where he was "alone, cold and hungry", a place that had few polite words for French refugees. Werth appears in the preamble to the novella, where Saint-Exupéry dedicates the book to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saint-Exup.C3.A9ry.a_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saint-Exup.C3.A9ry.a-69"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> It reads:</p>
<table class="cquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
<tr>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">“</td>
<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">
<p><tt><i>To Leon Werth</i></tt></p>
<p><tt><i>I ask children to forgive me for dedicating this book to a grown-up. I have a serious excuse: this grown-up is the best friend I have in the world. I have another excuse: this grown-up can understand everything, even books for children. I have a third excuse: he lives in France where he is hungry and cold. He needs to be comforted. If all these excuses are not enough then I want to dedicate this book to the child whom this grown-up once was. All grown-ups were children first. (But few of them remember it.) So I correct my dedication:</i></tt></p>
<p><tt><i>To Leon Werth,</i><br/>
<i>When he was a little boy</i></tt></p>
</td>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">”</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Saint-Exupéry's aircraft disappeared over the Mediterranean in July 1944. The following month, Werth learned of his friend's disappearance from a radio broadcast. Without having yet heard of <i>The Little Prince,</i> in November, Werth discovered that Saint-Exupéry had published a fable the previous year in the U.S., which he had illustrated himself, and that it was dedicated to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> At the end of the Second World War, which Antoine de Saint-Exupéry didn't live to see, Werth said: "Peace, without Tonio (Saint-Exupéry) isn't entirely peace." Werth did not see the text for which he was so responsible until five months after his friend's death, when Saint-Exupéry's French publisher, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89ditions_Gallimard" title="Éditions Gallimard">Gallimard</a>, sent him a special edition. Werth died in Paris in 1955.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Illustrations">Illustrations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Illustrations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>All of the novella's simple but elegant <a href="/wiki/Watercolor_painting" title="Watercolor painting">watercolour illustrations</a>, which were integral to the story, were painted by Saint-Exupéry. He had studied architecture as a young adult but nevertheless could not be considered an artist — which he self-mockingly alluded to in the novella's introduction. Several of his illustrations were painted on the wrong side of the delicate onion skin paper that he used, his medium of choice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2000.09.10_51-5"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2000.09.10-51"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> As with some of his draft manuscripts, he occasionally gave away preliminary sketches to close friends and colleagues; others were even recovered as crumpled balls from the floors in the cockpits he flew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>Note 10<span>]</span></a></sup> Two or three original <i>Little Prince</i> drawings were reported in the collections of New York artist, sculptor and experimental filmmaker <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Cornell" title="Joseph Cornell">Joseph Cornell</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Life-1967.12.15_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Life-1967.12.15-72"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> One rare original <i>Little Prince</i> watercolour would be mysteriously sold at a second-hand book fair in Japan in 1994, and subsequently authenticated in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Globe.26Mail-2006-04-07_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Globe.26Mail-2006-04-07-73"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CBC-2007.04.07_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-CBC-2007.04.07-74"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>An unrepentant lifelong doodler and sketcher, Saint-Exupéry had for many years sketched little people on his napkins, tablecloths, letters to paramours and friends, lined notebooks and other scraps of paper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrainPickings_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-BrainPickings-45"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-1993.05.30_47-3"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1993.05.30-47"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> Early figures took on a multitude of appearances, engaged in a variety of tasks. Some appeared as doll-like figures, baby puffins, angels with wings, and even a figure similar to that in <a href="/wiki/Robert_Crumb" title="Robert Crumb">Robert Crumb</a>'s later famous <a href="/wiki/Keep_on_Truckin%27_(comics)" title="Keep on Truckin' (comics)">Keep On Truckin'</a> of 1968. In a 1940 letter to a friend he sketched a character with his own thinning hair, sporting a bow tie, viewed as a boyish alter-ego, and he later gave a similar doodle to Elizabeth Reynal at his New York publisher's office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrainPickings_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-BrainPickings-45"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> Most often the diminutive figure was expressed as "...a slip of a boy with a turned up nose, lots of hair, long baggy pants that were too short for him and with a long scarf that whipped in the wind. Usually the boy had a puzzled expression... [T]his boy Saint-Exupéry came to think of as "the little prince," and he was usually found standing on top of a tiny planet. Most of the time he was alone, sometimes walking up a path. Sometimes there was a single flower on the planet."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-1942.12.06_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1942.12.06-57"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> His characters were frequently seen chasing butterflies; when asked why they did so, Saint-Exupéry, who thought of the figures as his alter-egos, replied that they were actually pursuing a "realistic ideal".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-1993.05.30_47-4"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1993.05.30-47"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> Saint-Exupéry eventually settled on the image of the young, precocious child with curly blond hair, an image which would become the subject of speculations as to its source. One "most striking" illustration depicted the pilot-narrator asleep beside his stranded plane prior to the prince's arrival. Although images of the narrator were created for the story, none survived Saint-Exupéry's editing process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fast_Company-2014.01.31_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-Fast_Company-2014.01.31-7"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>To mark both the 50th and 70th anniversaries of <i>The Little Prince's</i> publication, the <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Library_%26_Museum" title="Morgan Library &amp; Museum">Morgan Library and Museum</a> mounted major exhibitions of Saint-Exupéry's draft manuscript, preparatory drawings, and similar materials that it had obtained earlier from a variety of sources. One major source was an intimate friend of his in New York City, Silvia Hamilton (later, Reinhardt), to whom the author gave his working manuscript just prior to returning to Algiers to resume his work as a <a href="/wiki/Free_French_Air_Force" title="Free French Air Force">Free French Air Force</a> pilot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-1993.09.19_34-4"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1993.09.19-34"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2014.01.23.a_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2014.01.23.a-65"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Telegraph-2014.01.24_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Telegraph-2014.01.24-75"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> Hamilton's black <a href="/wiki/Poodle" title="Poodle">poodle</a>, Mocha, is believed to have been the model for the Little Prince's sheep, with a <a href="/wiki/Raggedy_Ann" title="Raggedy Ann">Raggedy Ann</a> type doll helping as a stand-in for the prince.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fine_Books_Magazine-2013.12.03_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fine_Books_Magazine-2013.12.03-64"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> Additionally, a pet <a href="/wiki/Boxer_(dog)" title="Boxer (dog)">boxer</a>, Hannibal, that Hamilton gave to him as a gift may have been the model for the story's desert fox and its tiger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wall_Street_Journal-2014.01.23_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wall_Street_Journal-2014.01.23-14"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> A museum representative stated that the novella's final drawings were lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-1993.09.19_34-5"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1993.09.19-34"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Seven unpublished drawings for the book were also displayed at the museum's exhibit, including fearsome looking baobab trees ready to destroy the prince's home asteroid, as well as a picture of the story's narrator, the forlorn pilot, sleeping next to his aircraft. That image was likely omitted to avoid giving the story a 'literalness' that would distract its readers, according to one of the Morgan Library's staff.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-1993.09.19_34-6"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1993.09.19-34"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> According to Christine Nelson, curator of literary and historical manuscripts at the Morgan, "[t]he image evokes Saint-Exupéry's own experience of awakening in an isolated, mysterious place. You can almost imagine him wandering without much food and water and conjuring up the character of the Little Prince."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fast_Company-2014.01.31_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-Fast_Company-2014.01.31-7"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> Another reviewer noted that the author "...chose the best illustrations... to maintain the ethereal tone he wanted his story to exude. Choosing between ambiguity and literal text and illustrations, Saint-Exupéry chose in every case to obfuscate."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EphemeralPursuits_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-EphemeralPursuits-76"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> Not a single drawing of the story's narrator–pilot survived the author's editing process; "...he was very good at excising what was not essential to his story".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fast_Company-2014.01.31_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-Fast_Company-2014.01.31-7"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 2001 Japanese researcher Yoshitsugu Kunugiyama surmised that the cover illustration Saint-Exupéry painted for <i>Le Petit Prince</i> deliberately depicted a stellar arrangement created to celebrate the author's own centennial of birth. According to Kunugiyama, the cover art chosen from one of Saint-Exupéry's watercolour illustrations contained the planets <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>, plus the star <a href="/wiki/Aldebaran" title="Aldebaran">Aldebaran</a>, arranged as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scalene_triangle" title="Scalene triangle">isosceles triangle</a>, a celestial configuration which occurred in the early 1940s, and which he likely knew would next reoccur in the year 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shimbun_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shimbun-77"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> Saint-Exupéry possessed superior mathematical skills and was a master <a href="/wiki/Celestial_navigation" title="Celestial navigation">celestial navigator</a>, a vocation he had studied at <a href="/wiki/Salon-de-Provence_Air_Base" title="Salon-de-Provence Air Base">Salon-de-Provence</a> with the <a href="/wiki/French_Air_Force" title="French Air Force"><i>Armée de l'Air</i></a> (French Air Force).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-publication">Post-publication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Post-publication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Stacy_Schiff" title="Stacy Schiff">Stacy Schiff</a>, one of <a href="/wiki/Antoine_de_Saint-Exup%C3%A9ry" title="Antoine de Saint-Exupéry">Saint-Exupéry</a>'s principal biographers, wrote of him and his most famous work, "rarely have an author and a character been so intimately bound together as Antoine de Saint-Exupéry and his Little Prince", and remarking of their dual fates, "...the two remain tangled together, twin innocents who fell from the sky".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2000.06.25_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2000.06.25-78"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> Another noted that the novella's mystique was "...enhanced by the parallel between author and subject: imperious innocents whose lives consist of equal parts flight and failed love, who fall to earth, are little impressed with what they find here and ultimately disappear without a trace."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2004.04.11_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2004.04.11-79"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Only weeks after his novella was first published in April 1943, despite his wife's pleadings and before Saint-Exupéry had received any of its royalties (he never would), the author-aviator joined the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_French_Forces" title="Free French Forces">Free French Forces</a>. He would remain immensely proud of <i>The Little Prince</i>, and almost always kept a personal copy with him which he often read to others during the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2000.06.25_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2000.06.25-78"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As part of a 32 ship military convoy he voyaged to <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a> where he rejoined his old squadron to fight with the Allies, resuming his work as a reconnaissance pilot despite the best efforts of his friends, colleagues and fellow airmen who could not prevent him from flying.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span>[</span>Note 11<span>]</span></a></sup> He had previously escaped death by the barest of margins a number of times, but was then <a href="/wiki/Antoine_de_Saint-Exup%C3%A9ry#Disappearance" title="Antoine de Saint-Exupéry">lost in action</a> during a July 1944 spy mission from the moonscapes of <a href="/wiki/Corsica" title="Corsica">Corsica</a> to <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">the continent</a> in preparation for the <a href="/wiki/Operation_Dragoon" title="Operation Dragoon">Allied invasion of occupied France</a>, only three weeks before the <a href="/wiki/Liberation_of_Paris" title="Liberation of Paris">Liberation of Paris</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-1993.05.30_47-5"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1993.05.30-47"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>Note 12<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd><i>Further information: <a href="/wiki/Antoine_de_Saint-Exup%C3%A9ry#Disappearance" title="Antoine de Saint-Exupéry">Antoine de Saint-Exupéry –Disappearance</a></i></dd>
</dl>
