<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">Reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many of the book's initial reviewers were flummoxed by the fable's multi-layered story line and its morals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_Yorker-2014.04.29_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-New_Yorker-2014.04.29-16"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> perhaps expecting a significantly more conventional story from one of France's leading writers. Its publisher had anticipated such reactions to a work that fell neither exclusively into a children's or adult's literature classification. The <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">New York Times</a></i> wrote shortly before its release "What makes a good children's book?.... ...<i>The Little Prince</i>, which is a fascinating fable for grown-ups [is] of conjectural value for boys and girls of 6, 8 and 10. [It] may very well be a book on the order of <i><a href="/wiki/Gulliver%27s_Travels" title="Gulliver's Travels">Gulliver's Travels</a></i>, something that exists on two levels"; "Can you clutter up a narrative with paradox and irony and still hold the interest of 8 and 10 year olds?" Notwithstanding the story's duality, the review added that major portions of the story would probably still "capture the imagination of any child."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-1943.04.06_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1943.04.06-84"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> Addressing whether it was written for children or adults, <a href="/wiki/Reynal_%26_Hitchcock" title="Reynal &amp; Hitchcock">Reynal &amp; Hitchcock</a> promoted it ambiguously, saying that as far as they were concerned "it's the new book by Saint-Exupéry", adding to its dustcover "There are few stories which in some way, in some degree, change the world forever for their readers. This is one."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ThreeTomatoes_62-3"><a href="#cite_note-ThreeTomatoes-62"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Others were not shy in offering their praise. Austin Stevens, also of <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>, stated that the story possessed "...large portions of the Saint-Exupéry philosophy and poetic spirit. In a way it's a sort of <a href="/wiki/Credo" title="Credo">credo</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-1942.12.06_57-3"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1942.12.06-57"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/P._L._Travers" title="P. L. Travers">P.L. Travers</a>, author of the <a href="/wiki/Mary_Poppins" title="Mary Poppins">Mary Poppins</a> series of children books, wrote in a <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Herald_Tribune" title="New York Herald Tribune">Herald Tribune</a></i> review: "...<i>The Little Prince</i> will shine upon children with a sidewise gleam. It will strike them in some place that is not the mind and glow there until the time comes for them to comprehend it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ThreeTomatoes_62-4"><a href="#cite_note-ThreeTomatoes-62"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2014.01.23.b_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2014.01.23.b-85"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>British journalist Neil Clark, in <i><a href="/wiki/The_American_Conservative" title="The American Conservative">The American Conservative</a></i>, much later offered an expansive view of Saint-Exupéry's overall work by commenting that it provides a "…bird's eye view of humanity [and] contains some of the most profound observations on the human condition ever written", and that the author's novella "…doesn't merely express his contempt for selfishness and materialism [but] shows how life should be lived."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-American_Conservative-2009.10_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-American_Conservative-2009.10-86"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The book enjoyed only modest initial success, residing on the <a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times_Best_Seller_list" title="The New York Times Best Seller list"><i>The New York Times</i> Best Seller list</a> for only two weeks,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-La_Presse-2014.01.27_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-La_Presse-2014.01.27-66"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> as opposed to his earlier 1939 English translation, <i><a href="/wiki/Wind,_Sand_and_Stars" title="Wind, Sand and Stars">Wind, Sand and Stars</a></i> which remained on the same list for nearly five months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrainPickings_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-BrainPickings-45"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> As a cultural icon, the novella regularly draws new readers and reviewers, selling almost two million copies annually and also spawning <a href="/wiki/List_of_The_Little_Prince_adaptations" title="List of The Little Prince adaptations">numerous adaptations</a>. Modern-day references to <i>The Little Prince</i> include one from <i>The New York Times</i> that describes it as "abstract" and "fabulistic".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2014.01.23.a_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2014.01.23.a-65"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_science_fiction">As science fiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: As science fiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Saint-Exupéry made no attempt at scientific accuracy, and the asteroid on which the Little Prince lives bears little resemblance to an actual <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a>. Nevertheless, in retrospect, his book could be considered a pioneering work in depicting humans living on asteroids, a theme which would become a staple of many later works of <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Asteroids_in_fiction" title="Asteroids in fiction">Asteroids in fiction</a>).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_translations_and_printed_editions">Literary translations and printed editions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Literary translations and printed editions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saint_Exupery_exhibit_-_Air_%26_Space_Museum,_Le_Bourget,_Paris,_France,_absent_artwork_and_not_subject_to_copyright.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Saint_Exupery_exhibit_-_Air_%26_Space_Museum%2C_Le_Bourget%2C_Paris%2C_France%2C_absent_artwork_and_not_subject_to_copyright.jpg/220px-Saint_Exupery_exhibit_-_Air_%26_Space_Museum%2C_Le_Bourget%2C_Paris%2C_France%2C_absent_artwork_and_not_subject_to_copyright.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Saint_Exupery_exhibit_-_Air_%26_Space_Museum%2C_Le_Bourget%2C_Paris%2C_France%2C_absent_artwork_and_not_subject_to_copyright.jpg/330px-Saint_Exupery_exhibit_-_Air_%26_Space_Museum%2C_Le_Bourget%2C_Paris%2C_France%2C_absent_artwork_and_not_subject_to_copyright.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Saint_Exupery_exhibit_-_Air_%26_Space_Museum%2C_Le_Bourget%2C_Paris%2C_France%2C_absent_artwork_and_not_subject_to_copyright.jpg/440px-Saint_Exupery_exhibit_-_Air_%26_Space_Museum%2C_Le_Bourget%2C_Paris%2C_France%2C_absent_artwork_and_not_subject_to_copyright.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saint_Exupery_exhibit_-_Air_%26_Space_Museum,_Le_Bourget,_Paris,_France,_absent_artwork_and_not_subject_to_copyright.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Two editions of <i>The Little Prince</i> (lower left in French and upper right in English, artwork not shown) in the Saint-Exupéry permanent exhibit at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_Air_and_Space_Museum" title="French Air and Space Museum">French Air and Space Museum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Le_Bourget" title="Le Bourget">Le Bourget, Paris</a> (2008).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:National_Museum_of_Ethnology,_Osaka_-_Collection_of_%22Le_Petit_Prince%22_in_many_languages.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3096" data-file-width="4128" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/National_Museum_of_Ethnology%2C_Osaka_-_Collection_of_%22Le_Petit_Prince%22_in_many_languages.jpg/220px-National_Museum_of_Ethnology%2C_Osaka_-_Collection_of_%22Le_Petit_Prince%22_in_many_languages.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/National_Museum_of_Ethnology%2C_Osaka_-_Collection_of_%22Le_Petit_Prince%22_in_many_languages.jpg/330px-National_Museum_of_Ethnology%2C_Osaka_-_Collection_of_%22Le_Petit_Prince%22_in_many_languages.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/National_Museum_of_Ethnology%2C_Osaka_-_Collection_of_%22Le_Petit_Prince%22_in_many_languages.jpg/440px-National_Museum_of_Ethnology%2C_Osaka_-_Collection_of_%22Le_Petit_Prince%22_in_many_languages.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:National_Museum_of_Ethnology,_Osaka_-_Collection_of_%22Le_Petit_Prince%22_in_many_languages.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Some of the more than 250 translations of <i>The Little Prince</i>, these editions displayed at the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Ethnology_(Japan)" title="National Museum of Ethnology (Japan)">National Museum of Ethnology</a>, Osaka, Japan (2013).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Katherine Woods (1886–1968)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MorganLibrary_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-MorganLibrary-87"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> produced the classic English translation of 1943 which was later joined by several other English translations. Her original version contained some errors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoodTranslationGuide_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-GoodTranslationGuide-88"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cjvlang2_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-cjvlang2-89"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> Mistranslations aside, one reviewer noted that Wood's almost "poetic" English translation has long been admired by many <i>Little Prince</i> lovers who have spanned generations (it stayed in print until 2001), as her work maintains Saint-Exupéry's story-telling spirit and charm, if not its literal accuracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EphemeralPursuits_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-EphemeralPursuits-76"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> As of 2014<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> at least six additional English translations have been published:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patoche_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Patoche-90"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>T.V.F. Cuffe, (<a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0141185627">ISBN 0-14-118562-7</a>, 1st ed. 1995)</li>
<li>Irene Testot-Ferry, (<a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0756751896">ISBN 0-7567-5189-6</a>, 1st ed. 1995)</li>
<li>Alan Wakeman, (<a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/186205066X">ISBN 1-86205-066-X</a>, 1st ed. 1995)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wakeman_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wakeman-91"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Richard_Howard" title="Richard Howard">Richard Howard</a>, (<a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0152048049">ISBN 0-15-204804-9</a>, 1st ed. 2000)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NST_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-NST-3"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Ros and Chloe Schwartz, (<a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781907360015">ISBN 9781907360015</a>, 1st ed. 2010)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BookDepository.co.uk_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-BookDepository.co.uk-92"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>David Wilkinson, (bilingual English-French student edition, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0956721591">ISBN 0-9567215-9-1</a>, 1st ed. 2011)</li>
</ul>
<p>Each of these translators approaches the essence of the original with his or her own style and focus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cjvlang_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-cjvlang-93"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-editoreric_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-editoreric-94"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><i>Le Petit Prince</i> is often used as a beginner's book for <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French language</a> students, and several bilingual and trilingual translations have been published. As of 2014 it has been translated into more than 250 languages and dialects, including <a href="/wiki/Sardinian_language" title="Sardinian language">Sardinian</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elpetitprincep_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-elpetitprincep-95"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Constructed_language" title="Constructed language">constructed international language</a> of <a href="/wiki/Esperanto" title="Esperanto">Esperanto</a>, and the Congolese language <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alur_language" title="Alur language">Alur</a>, as well as being printed in <a href="/wiki/Braille" title="Braille">braille</a> for <a href="/wiki/Blindness" title="Blindness">visually impaired</a> readers. It is one of the few modern books to have been translated into <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, as <i>Regulus vel Pueri Soli Sapiunt</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hinke-Quand_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hinke-Quand-96"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LA_Times-1993.09.29_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-LA_Times-1993.09.29-97"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2005, the book was also translated into <a href="/wiki/Toba_Qom_language" title="Toba Qom language">Toba</a>, an indigenous language of northern <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, as <i>So Shiyaxauolec Nta'a</i>. It was the first book translated into this language since the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> of the Bible. Anthropologist Florence Tola, commenting on the suitability of the work for Toban translation, said there is "nothing strange [when] the Little Prince speaks with a snake or a fox and travels among the stars, it fits perfectly into the Toba mythology."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Le_Monde-2005.04.06_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Le_Monde-2005.04.06-98"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linguist" title="Linguist">Linguists</a> have compared the many translations and even editions of the same translation for style, composition, titles, wordings and genealogy. As an example: as of 2011 there are approximately 47 translated editions of <i>The Little Prince</i> in <a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span>[</span>Note 13<span>]</span></a></sup> and there are also about 50 different translated editions in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a> (produced in both mainland China and Taiwan). Many of them are titled <i>Prince From a Star</i>, while others carry the book title that is a direct translation of <i>The Little Prince</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bathrobe-a_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bathrobe-a-101"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> By studying the use of word phrasings, nouns, mistranslations and other content in newer editions, linguists can identify the source material for each version: whether it was derived from the original French typescript, or from its first translation into English by Katherine Woods, or from a number of adapted sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EphemeralPursuits_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-EphemeralPursuits-76"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bathrobe-b_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bathrobe-b-102"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The first edition to be published in France, Saint-Exupéry's birthplace, would not be printed by his regular publisher in that country, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89ditions_Gallimard" title="Éditions Gallimard">Gallimard</a>, until after the <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">Second World War</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LePetitPrince.net.b_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-LePetitPrince.net.b-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> as the author's blunt views within his eloquent writings were soon banned by the German's Nazi appeasers in <a href="/wiki/Vichy_France" title="Vichy France">Vichy France</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span>[</span>Note 14<span>]</span></a></sup> Prior to <a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_France_during_World_War_II#Campaign_of_France_.281944.E2.80.931945.29" title="Military history of France during World War II">France's liberation</a> new printings of Saint-Exupéry's works were made available only by means of secret print runs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeverson2004166.2C_171_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeverson2004166.2C_171-106"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchiff1996366_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchiff1996366-107"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> such as that of February 1943 when 1,000 copies of an underground version of his best seller <i>Pilote de guerre</i>, describing the German invasion of France, were covertly printed in Lyon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SaintExChronoOfPublications_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-SaintExChronoOfPublications-108"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Commemorating the novella's 70th anniversary of publication, in conjunction with the <a href="/wiki/The_Little_Prince#Morgan_exhibitions" title="The Little Prince">2014 Morgan Exhibition</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89ditions_Gallimard" title="Éditions Gallimard">Éditions Gallimard</a> released a complete facsimile edition of Saint-Exupéry's original handwritten manuscript entitled <i>Le Manuscrit du Petit Prince d'Antoine de Saint-Exupéry: Facsimilé et Transcription</i>, edited by Alban Cerisier and Delphine Lacroix. The book in its final form has also been republished in 70th anniversary editions by <a href="/wiki/Houghton_Mifflin_Harcourt" title="Houghton Mifflin Harcourt">Houghton Mifflin Harcourt</a> (in English) and by Gallimard (in French).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fine_Books_Magazine-2013.12.03_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-Fine_Books_Magazine-2013.12.03-64"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spanish_editions">Spanish editions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Spanish editions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After being translated by <a class="extiw" href="//es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bonifacio_del_Carril" title="es:Bonifacio del Carril">Bonifacio del Carril</a>, <i>The Little Prince</i> was first published in <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a> as <a class="extiw" href="//es.wikipedia.org/wiki/El_principito" title="es:El principito"><i>El principito</i></a> in September 1951 by the <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentine</a> publisher <a class="extiw" href="//es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Emec%C3%A9_Editores" title="es:Emecé Editores">Emecé Editores</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LePetitPrince.net.a_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-LePetitPrince.net.a-109"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LePetitPrince.net.c_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-LePetitPrince.net.c-110"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> Other Spanish editions have also been created; in 1956 the <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexican</a> publisher Diana released its first edition of the book, <i>El pequeño príncipe</i>, a Spanish translation by José María Francés.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LePetitPrince.net.b_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-LePetitPrince.net.b-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> Another edition of the work was produced in Spain in 1964 and, four years later, in 1968, editions were also produced in <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>, with translation by Luis Fernández in 1961. <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a> had its first translation in 1981; <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a> in February 1985; <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a> in 1986, and <a href="/wiki/Uruguay" title="Uruguay">Uruguay</a> in 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LePetitPrince.net.a_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-LePetitPrince.net.a-109"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LePetitPrince.net.e_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-LePetitPrince.net.e-111"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LePetitPrince.net.d_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-LePetitPrince.net.d-112"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extension_of_copyrights_in_France">Extension of copyrights in France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Extension of copyrights in France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Due to Saint-Exupéry's wartime death, his estate received the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleonic_code" title="Napoleonic code">civil code</a> designation <i><a href="/wiki/Mort_pour_la_France" title="Mort pour la France">Mort pour la France</a></i> (English: <i>Died for France</i>), which was applied by the French Government in 1948. Amongst the law's provisions is an increase of 30 years in the duration of copyright;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Celog.fr_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Celog.fr-113"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> thus most of Saint-Exupéry's creative works will not fall out of copyright status in France for an extra 30 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchiff2006438_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchiff2006438-114"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2004.04.11_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2004.04.11-79"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Adaptations_and_sequels">Adaptations and sequels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Adaptations and sequels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Richard_Burton_narrating_%27The_Little_Prince%27,_short_45_RPM_demo_excerpt.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="517" data-file-width="550" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Richard_Burton_narrating_%27The_Little_Prince%27%2C_short_45_RPM_demo_excerpt.jpg/220px-Richard_Burton_narrating_%27The_Little_Prince%27%2C_short_45_RPM_demo_excerpt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Richard_Burton_narrating_%27The_Little_Prince%27%2C_short_45_RPM_demo_excerpt.jpg/330px-Richard_Burton_narrating_%27The_Little_Prince%27%2C_short_45_RPM_demo_excerpt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Richard_Burton_narrating_%27The_Little_Prince%27%2C_short_45_RPM_demo_excerpt.jpg/440px-Richard_Burton_narrating_%27The_Little_Prince%27%2C_short_45_RPM_demo_excerpt.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Richard_Burton_narrating_%27The_Little_Prince%27,_short_45_RPM_demo_excerpt.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A short <a href="/wiki/Single_(music)#Types" title="Single (music)">45 RPM demo recording</a> by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Burton" title="Richard Burton">Richard Burton</a> narrating <i>The Little Prince</i> with music by <a href="/wiki/Mort_Garson" title="Mort Garson">Mort Garson</a>, excerpted from a longer 33⅓ RPM <a href="/wiki/LP_record" title="LP record">vinyl record album</a>. Burton won the <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Best_Album_for_Children" title="Grammy Award for Best Album for Children">Best Children's Album Grammy Award</a> for his narration (1975).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_The_Little_Prince_adaptations" title="List of The Little Prince adaptations">List of The Little Prince adaptations</a></div>
<p>The wide appeal of Saint-Exupéry's novella has led to it being adapted into numerous forms over the decades. Additionally, the little prince character himself has been adapted to a number of promotional roles, including as a symbol of <a href="/wiki/Environmental_protection" title="Environmental protection">environmental protection</a>, by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toshiba_Group" title="Toshiba Group">Toshiba Group</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeaumont2011_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeaumont2011-115"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> He has also been portrayed as a "virtual ambassador" in a <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_control_movement" title="Tobacco control movement">campaign against smoking</a>, employed by the <a href="/wiki/Veolia_Environnement" title="Veolia Environnement">Veolia Energy Services Group</a>, <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeaumont2011_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeaumont2011-115"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> and his name was used as an episode title in the TV series <i><a href="/wiki/Lost_(TV_series)" title="Lost (TV series)">Lost</a></i>.</p>
<p>The multi-layered fable, styled as a children's story with its philosophical elements of irony and paradox directed towards adults, allowed <i>The Little Prince</i> to be transferred into various other <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">art forms</a> and media, including:</p>
<ul>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vinyl_record" title="Vinyl record">Vinyl record</a>, <a href="/wiki/Compact_Cassette" title="Compact Cassette">cassette</a> and <a href="/wiki/Compact_disc" title="Compact disc">CD</a>: as early as 1954 several audio editions in multiple languages were created on vinyl record, cassette tape and much latter as a CD, with one English version narrated by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Burton" title="Richard Burton">Richard Burton</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Radio_broadcasting" title="Radio broadcasting">Radio broadcasts</a>: radio plays were produced in the United States, with <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Burr" title="Raymond Burr">Raymond Burr</a>, in 1956 and by <a href="/wiki/Bonnie_Greer" title="Bonnie Greer">Bonnie Greer</a> on the United Kingdom's <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio" title="BBC Radio">BBC</a> in 2000.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Film" title="Film">Film</a> and <a href="/wiki/Television" title="Television">TV</a>: the story has been created as a movie as early as 1966 in a Soviet-Lithuanian production, with its first English movie version in 1974 produced in the United States featuring <a href="/wiki/Bob_Fosse" title="Bob Fosse">Bob Fosse</a>, who choreographed his own dance sequence as The Snake, and <a href="/wiki/Gene_Wilder" title="Gene Wilder">Gene Wilder</a> as The Fox. A <a href="/wiki/The_Little_Prince_(2015_film)" title="The Little Prince (2015 film)">new 3D film</a> combining computer and stop motion animation will be released in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Movieweb.com_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Movieweb.com-116"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollyWoodReporter.com_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-HollyWoodReporter.com-117"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Theatre" title="Theatre">Stage</a>: <i>The Little Prince's</i> popular appeal has lent itself to widespread dramatic adaptations in live stage productions at both the professional and amateur levels. It has become a staple of numerous stage companies, with dozens of productions created.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Graphic_novel" title="Graphic novel">Graphic novel</a>: a new printed version of the story in comic book form, by <a href="/wiki/Joann_Sfar" title="Joann Sfar">Joann Sfar</a> in 2008, drew widespread notice.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pop-Up_Book" title="Pop-Up Book">Pop-Up Book</a>: a new printed edition, using the original text (as translated by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Howard" title="Richard Howard">Richard Howard</a> in 2000) and St. Exupery's original drawings as the basis for elaborate pop-up illustrations, was published by Houghton Mifflin Harcourt (<a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780547260693">ISBN 978-0-547-26069-3</a>, 1st ed. 2009).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Opera" title="Opera">Opera</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ballet" title="Ballet">ballet</a>: several operatic and ballet versions of the novella have been produced as early as the Russian <i>Malen′kiy</i>, first performed in 1978 with a symphony score composed in the 1960s.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Anime" title="Anime">Anime</a>: a Japanese animation TV series was made in 1978 <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Hoshi_no_Ojisama_Petit_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Hoshi no Ojisama Petit Prince (page does not exist)">Hoshi no Ojisama Petit Prince</a>, contained 39 episodes that does not follow the plot of the original novella. Each episode would contain an adventure for planet Earth where the little prince meets different people each time, and make friends. Some key elements of the original story has been kept. Namely, the little prince's golden hair, his scarf and laughter. Also, His planet name (B-612), the rose and the three volcanoes. The anime had been aired and dubbed into many languages: Arabic, French, German, Italian, Polish, Portuguese and Spanish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Animenewsnetwork.com_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Animenewsnetwork.com-118"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Other: a number of musical references, game boards and a video game version of the novella have been released.</li>
</ul>
<p>In 1997, Jean-Pierre Davidts wrote what could be considered a sequel to <i>The Little Prince</i>, entitled <i>Le petit prince retrouvé</i> (<i>The Little Prince Returns</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davidts_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davidts-119"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> In this version, the narrator is a shipwrecked man who encounters the little prince on a lone island; the prince has returned to find help against a tiger who threatens his sheep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SDM.qc_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-SDM.qc-120"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> Another sequel titled <i>The Return of the Little Prince</i> was written by former actress Ysatis de Saint-Simone, niece of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Consuelo_de_Saint_Exupery" title="Consuelo de Saint Exupery">Consuelo de Saint Exupery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saint-Simone_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saint-Simone-121"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Honours_and_legacy">Honours and legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Honours and legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Museums_and_exhibits">Museums and exhibits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Museums and exhibits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Morgan_exhibitions">Morgan exhibitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Morgan exhibitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>New York City's <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Library_%26_Museum" title="Morgan Library &amp; Museum">Morgan Library &amp; Museum</a> mounted three showings of <a href="/wiki/Antoine_de_Saint-Exup%C3%A9ry" title="Antoine de Saint-Exupéry">Antoine de Saint-Exupéry</a>'s original manuscript, with its first showing in 1994 on the occasion of the story's 50th anniversary of publication, followed by one celebrating the author's centennial of birth in 2000, with its last and largest exhibition in 2014 honouring the novella's 70th anniversary.</p>
<p>The 1994 exhibition displayed the original manuscript, translated by the museum's art historian Ruth Kraemer,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2005.10.16_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2005.10.16-122"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> as well as a number of the story's watercolours drawn from the Morgan's permanent collection. Also included with the exhibits was a 20-minute video it produced, <i>My Grown-Up Friend, Saint-Exupéry,</i> narrated by actor <a href="/wiki/Macaulay_Culkin" title="Macaulay Culkin">Macaulay Culkin</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span>[</span>Note 15<span>]</span></a></sup> along with photos of the author, correspondence to <a href="/wiki/Consuelo_de_Saint_Exup%C3%A9ry" title="Consuelo de Saint Exupéry">his wife Consuelo</a>, a signed first edition of <i>The Little Prince</i>, and several international editions in other languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LA_Times-1993.09.29_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-LA_Times-1993.09.29-97"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In January 2014 the museum mounted a third, significantly larger exhibition centered on the novella's creative origins and its history. The major showing of <i>The Little Prince: A New York Story</i> celebrated the story's 70th anniversary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2014.01.23.a_65-3"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2014.01.23.a-65"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> It examined both the novella's New York origins and Saint-Exupéry's creative processes, looking at his story and paintings as they evolved from conceptual germ form into progressively more refined versions, and finally into the book's highly polished first edition. "The exhibition allows us to step back to the moment of creation and witness Saint-Exupéry at work..." wrote the museum's director, William Griswold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daily_Mail-2014.02.11_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-Daily_Mail-2014.02.11-19"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> It was if visitors were able to look over his shoulder as he worked, according to curator Christine Nelson. Funding for the 2014 exhibition was provided by several benefactors, including The Florence Gould Foundation, The Caroline Macomber Fund, <a href="/wiki/Houghton_Mifflin_Harcourt" title="Houghton Mifflin Harcourt">Houghton Mifflin Harcourt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Air_France" title="Air France">Air France</a> and the <a href="/wiki/New_York_State_Council_on_the_Arts" title="New York State Council on the Arts">New York State Council on the Arts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fine_Books_Magazine-2013.12.03_64-3"><a href="#cite_note-Fine_Books_Magazine-2013.12.03-64"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The new, more comprehensive exhibits included 35 <a href="/wiki/Watercolor_painting" title="Watercolor painting">watercolor paintings</a> and 25 of the work's original 140 handwritten manuscript pages,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-La_Presse-2014.01.06_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-La_Presse-2014.01.06-125"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> with his almost illegible handwriting penciled onto 'Fidelity' watermarked <a href="/wiki/Onionskin" title="Onionskin">onion skin paper</a>. The autograph manuscript pages included <a href="/wiki/Strikethrough" title="Strikethrough">struck-through</a> content that was not published in the novella's first edition. As well, some 43 preparatory pencil drawings that evolved into the story's illustrations accompanied the manuscript, many of them dampened by moisture that rippled its onion skin media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Metropolis_Magazine-2014.01.29_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Metropolis_Magazine-2014.01.29-126"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Complex_magazine-2014.01.21_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Complex_magazine-2014.01.21-127"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup> One painting depicted the prince floating above Earth wearing a yellow scarf was wrinkled, having been crumpled up and thrown away before being retrieved for preservation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wall_Street_Journal-2014.01.23_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-Wall_Street_Journal-2014.01.23-14"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daily_Mail-2014.02.11_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-Daily_Mail-2014.02.11-19"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> Another drawing loaned from Silvia Hamilton's grandson depicted the diminutive prince observing a sunset on his home asteroid; two other versions of the same drawing were also displayed alongside it allowing visitors to observe the drawings progressive refinement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ThreeTomatoes_62-5"><a href="#cite_note-ThreeTomatoes-62"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> The initial working manuscript and sketches, displayed side-by-side with pages from the novella's first edition, allowed viewers to observe the evolution of Saint-Exupéry's work.</p>
<p>Shortly before departing the United States to rejoin his reconnaissance squadron in North Africa in its struggle against Nazi Germany, Saint-Exupéry appeared unexpectedly in military uniform at the door of his intimate friend Silvia Hamilton. He presented his working manuscript and its preliminary drawings in a "rumpled paper bag", placed onto her home's entryway table, offering "I'd like to give you something splendid, but this is all I have".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2014.01.23.a_65-4"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2014.01.23.a-65"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Telegraph-2014.01.24_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-Telegraph-2014.01.24-75"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2014.01.23.b_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2014.01.23.b-85"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Complex_magazine-2014.01.21_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-Complex_magazine-2014.01.21-127"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan_Museum-2014.01.main_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan_Museum-2014.01.main-128"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> Several of the manuscript pages bore accidental coffee stains and cigarette scorch marks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wall_Street_Journal-2014.01.23_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-Wall_Street_Journal-2014.01.23-14"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> The Morgan later acquired the 30,000 word manuscript from Hamilton in 1968, with its pages becoming the centrepieces of its exhibitions on Saint-Exupéry's work. The 2014 exhibition also borrowed artifacts and the author's personal letters from the Saint Exupéry-d'Gay Estate,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span>[</span>Note 16<span>]</span></a></sup> as well as materials from other private collections, libraries and museums in the United States and France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan_Library_.26_Museum_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan_Library_.26_Museum-130"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> Running concurrent with its 2014 exhibition, the Morgan held a series of lectures, concerts and film showings, including talks by Saint-Exupéry biographer <a href="/wiki/Stacy_Schiff" title="Stacy Schiff">Stacy Schiff</a>, writer <a href="/wiki/Adam_Gopnik" title="Adam Gopnik">Adam Gopnik</a>, and author <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peter_Sis" title="Peter Sis">Peter Sis</a> on his new work <i>The Pilot and The Little Prince: The Life of Antoine de Saint-Exupéry</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan_Museum-2014.01.main_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan_Museum-2014.01.main-128"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A.F.P.-2014.01.15_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-A.F.P.-2014.01.15-131"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Additional exhibits included photos of Saint-Exupéry by <a href="/wiki/Life_(magazine)" title="Life (magazine)">Life</a> photojournalist <a href="/wiki/John_Phillips_(photographer)" title="John Phillips (photographer)">John Phillips</a>, other photos of the author's New York area homes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fine_Books_Magazine-2013.12.03_64-4"><a href="#cite_note-Fine_Books_Magazine-2013.12.03-64"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> an <a href="/wiki/Orson_Welles" title="Orson Welles">Orson Welles</a> screenplay of the novella the filmmaker attempted to produce as a movie in collaboration with <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney" title="Walt Disney">Walt Disney</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrainPickings_45-4"><a href="#cite_note-BrainPickings-45"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2014.01.23.a_65-5"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2014.01.23.a-65"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132"><span>[</span>Note 17<span>]</span></a></sup> as well as one of the few signed copies extant of <i>The Little Prince</i>, gifted to Hamilton's 12 year old son.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133"><span>[</span>Note 18<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_B_612_Asteroid,_Hakone,_Japan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2462" data-file-width="1985" height="273" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/The_B_612_Asteroid%2C_Hakone%2C_Japan.jpg/220px-The_B_612_Asteroid%2C_Hakone%2C_Japan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/The_B_612_Asteroid%2C_Hakone%2C_Japan.jpg/330px-The_B_612_Asteroid%2C_Hakone%2C_Japan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/The_B_612_Asteroid%2C_Hakone%2C_Japan.jpg/440px-The_B_612_Asteroid%2C_Hakone%2C_Japan.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_B_612_Asteroid,_Hakone,_Japan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A tribute to <i>The Little Prince</i> atop Asteroid B-612, at the Museum of The Little Prince, Hakone, Japan (2007).</div>
</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Permanent_exhibits">Permanent exhibits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Permanent exhibits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Le_Bourget" title="Le Bourget">Le Bourget</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paris,_France" title="Paris, France">Paris, France</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_Air_and_Space_Museum" title="French Air and Space Museum">Air and Space Museum of France</a> established a special exhibit honoring Saint-Exupéry, and which displays many of his literary creations. Among them are various early editions of <i>The Little Prince</i>. Remnants of the <a href="/wiki/Free_French_Air_Force" title="Free French Air Force">Free French Air Force</a> <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_P-38_Lightning#Antoine_de_Saint-Exup.C3.A9ry" title="Lockheed P-38 Lightning">P-38 Lightning</a> in which he disappeared, and which were recovered from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a> in 2004, are also on view.</li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Hakone,_Kanagawa" title="Hakone, Kanagawa">Hakone</a>, Japan there is the Museum of The Little Prince featuring outdoor squares and sculptures such as the B-612 Asteroid, the Lamplighter Square, and a sculpture of the Little Prince. The museum grounds additionally feature a <i>Little Prince Park</i> along with the <i>Consuelo Rose Garden</i>; however the main portion of the museum are its indoor exhibits.</li>
<li>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gyeonggi-do" title="Gyeonggi-do">Gyeonggi-do</a>, South Korea, there is an imitation French village, Petite France, which has adapted the story elements of <i>The Little Prince</i> into its architecture and monuments. There are several sculptures of the story's characters, and the village also offers overnight housing in some of the French-style homes. Featured are the history of <i>The Little Prince</i>, an art gallery, and a small <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/amphitheatre" title="wikt:amphitheatre">amphitheatre</a> situated in the middle of the village for musicians and other performances. The enterprise's director stated that in 2009 the village received a half million visitors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SaintExup.C3.A9ry.com-a_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-SaintExup.C3.A9ry.com-a-99"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VisitKorea-2_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-VisitKorea-2-134"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VisitKorea_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-VisitKorea-135"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Special_exhibitions">Special exhibitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Special exhibitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul>
<li>In 1996 the Danish <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sculptor" title="Sculptor">sculptor</a> <a href="/wiki/Jens_Galschi%C3%B8t" title="Jens Galschiøt">Jens Galschiøt</a> unveiled an artistic arrangement consisting of seven blocks of granite asteroids 'floating' in a circle around a 2-metre tall planet Earth. The artistic universe was populated by bronze sculpture figures that the little prince met on his journeys. As in the book, the prince discovers that "the essential is invisible to the eye, and only by the heart can you really see". The work was completed at the start of 1996 and placed in the central square of <a href="/wiki/Fuglebjerg" title="Fuglebjerg">Fuglebjerg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Galschi.C3.B8tGallery_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Galschi.C3.B8tGallery-136"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup> but was later stolen from an exhibition in <a href="/wiki/Billund,_Denmark" title="Billund, Denmark">Billund</a> in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berlingske-2011.10.17_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berlingske-2011.10.17-137"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>During 2009 in <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo" title="São Paulo">São Paulo</a>, Brazil, the giant Oca Art Exhibition Centre presented The Little Prince as part of <i>'The Year of France and The Little Prince'.</i> The displays covered over 10,000 square metres on four floors, examining Saint-Exupéry, The Little Prince and their philosophies, as visitors passed through theme areas of the desert, different worlds, stars and the cosmos. The ground floor of the exhibit area was laid out as a huge map of the routes flown by the author and <a href="/wiki/A%C3%A9ropostale_(aviation)" title="Aéropostale (aviation)">Aeropostale</a> in South America and around the world. Also included was a full-scale replica of his <a href="/wiki/Caudron_Simoun" title="Caudron Simoun">Caudron Simoun</a>, crashed in a simulated Sahara Desert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TheLittlePrince.com-2011.03.14_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-TheLittlePrince.com-2011.03.14-138"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JovemMuseologia_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-JovemMuseologia-139"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carvalho_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carvalho-140"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>In 2012 the <a href="/wiki/Catalonia" title="Catalonia">Catalan</a> <a href="/wiki/Architect" title="Architect">architect</a> Jan Baca unvelied a sculpture in <a href="/wiki/Terrassa" title="Terrassa">Terrassa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catalonia" title="Catalonia">Catalonia</a> showing the Little Prince image along the sentence "It is only with the heart that one can see rightly; what is essential is invisible to the eye"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-llibrevell_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-llibrevell-141"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Numismatics_and_philatelic">Numismatics and philatelic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Numismatics and philatelic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:50francstexupery.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="187" data-file-width="300" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fb/50francstexupery.jpg/220px-50francstexupery.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/f/fb/50francstexupery.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/f/fb/50francstexupery.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:50francstexupery.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Little Prince at bottom, and a portrait of his creator on a French 50-franc banknote (1993).</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>Before France adopted the <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">euro</a> as its currency, Saint-Exupéry and drawings from <i>The Little Prince</i> were on the 50-<a href="/wiki/French_franc" title="French franc">franc</a> banknote; the artwork was by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swiss" title="Swiss">Swiss</a> designer Roger Pfund.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2000.06.25_78-3"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2000.06.25-78"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FrenchBankNotes_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FrenchBankNotes-142"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup> Among the anti-counterfeiting measures on the banknote was micro-printed text from <i>Le Petit Prince</i>, visible with a strong magnifying glass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USC_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-USC-143"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup> Additionally, a 100-franc commemorative coin was also released in 2000, with Saint-Exupéry's image on its <a href="/wiki/Obverse_and_reverse" title="Obverse and reverse">obverse</a>, and that of the Little Prince on its reverse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPR-2000.12.23_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-NPR-2000.12.23-144"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>In commemoration of the 50th anniversary of the writer's untimely death, Israel issued a stamp honoring "Saint-Ex" and <i>The Little Prince</i> in 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trussel-is_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-trussel-is-145"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Philately" title="Philately">Philatelic</a> tributes have been printed in at least 24 other countries as of 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trussel-all_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-trussel-all-146"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomy">Astronomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Astronomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/B612_Foundation" title="B612 Foundation">B612 Foundation</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Private_foundation_(United_States)" title="Private foundation (United States)">private foundation</a> created to track <a href="/wiki/Near-Earth_object" title="Near-Earth object">Near-Earth objects</a> that might pose a threat to Earth, and is dedicated to protecting the planet from <a href="/wiki/Impact_event" title="Impact event">asteroid strikes</a>, similar to the <a href="/wiki/Tunguska_event" title="Tunguska event">Tunguska event</a> of 1908. The <a href="/wiki/Private_foundation_(United_States)" title="Private foundation (United States)">private foundation</a> was founded by a group of U.S. scientists and astronauts, including Clark Chapman, <a href="/wiki/Piet_Hut" title="Piet Hut">Piet Hut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rusty_Schweickart" title="Rusty Schweickart">Rusty Schweickart</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ed_Lu" title="Ed Lu">Ed Lu</a> in October 2002. The non-profit organization is named in honour of the prince's home <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>An asteroid discovered in 1975, <a href="/wiki/2578_Saint-Exup%C3%A9ry" title="2578 Saint-Exupéry">2578 Saint-Exupéry</a>, was also named after the author of <i>The Little Prince</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Another asteroid discovered in 1993 was named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/46610_B%C3%A9sixdouze" title="46610 Bésixdouze">46610 Bésixdouze</a>, which is French for "B six twelve". The asteroid's number, 46610, becomes <tt>B612</tt> in <a href="/wiki/Hexadecimal" title="Hexadecimal">hexadecimal</a> notation. B-612 was the name of the prince's home asteroid.</li>
<li>In 2003 a small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asteroid_moon" title="Asteroid moon">asteroid moon</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petit-Prince_(asteroid)" title="Petit-Prince (asteroid)">Petit-Prince</a>, discovered earlier in 1998, was named in part after <i>The Little Prince</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-William_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-William-149"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Insignia_and_awards">Insignia and awards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Insignia and awards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Er-1-33.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="474" data-file-width="298" height="318" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Er-1-33.jpg/200px-Er-1-33.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f5/Er-1-33.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f5/Er-1-33.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Er-1-33.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The fighter jet insignia of the <a class="extiw" href="//fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Escadron_1/33_Belfort" title="fr:Escadron 1/33 Belfort"><i>Escadron de reconnaissance 1/33 Belfort</i></a>, bearing an image of the Little Prince at top.</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>Prior to its decommissioning in 2010, the <i><a class="extiw" href="//fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Escadron_de_reconnaissance_1/33_Belfort" title="fr:Escadron de reconnaissance 1/33 Belfort">GR I/33</a></i> (later renamed as the 1/33 Belfort Squadron), one of the <a href="/wiki/French_Air_Force" title="French Air Force">French Air Force</a> squadrons Saint-Exupéry flew with, adopted the image of the <i>Little Prince</i> as part of the squadron and tail insignia of its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dassault_Mirage" title="Dassault Mirage">Dassault Mirage</a> fighter jets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchiff1994445_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchiff1994445-150"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup> Some of the fastest jets in the world were flown with The Prince gazing over their pilots' shoulders.</li>
<li><i>The Little Prince Literary Award</i> for <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> fiction by writers under the age of 15, commemorating the title of Saint-Exupéry's famous work, was created in <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> by the Cheragh-e Motale'eh Literary Foundation. In 2012, some 250 works by young authors were submitted for first stage review according to the society's secretary Maryam Sistani, with the selection of the best three writers from 30 finalists being conducted in <a href="/wiki/Tehran" title="Tehran">Tehran</a> that September.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tehran_Times-2012.09.09_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tehran_Times-2012.09.09-151"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IRNA-2012.09.25_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-IRNA-2012.09.25-152"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Several other Little Prince Awards have also been established in Europe, meant to promote achievement and excellence in a variety of fields such as in assistance to <a href="/wiki/Autism" title="Autism">autistic children</a>, child literacy, <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_literature" title="Children's literature">children's literature</a> (by adults), <a href="/wiki/Puppetry" title="Puppetry">puppetry theatre</a> and theatre arts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TokudaBolnica.com_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-TokudaBolnica.com-153"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prize-Autism.eu_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prize-Autism.eu-154"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Datoteke_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-Datoteke-155"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Schools">Schools</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Schools">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><i>L'école Le Petit Prince</i> is the public elementary school in the small community of <a href="/wiki/Genech" title="Genech">Genech</a> in northern France, dedicated in 1994 upon the merger of two former schools. With nine classrooms and a library, its building overlooks the village's <i>Place Terre des Hommes</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup> a square also named in tribute to Saint-Exupéry's 1939 philosophical memoir, <i><a class="extiw" href="//fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terre_des_hommes" title="fr:Terre des hommes">Terre des hommes</a></i>.</li>
<li>A K–6 elementary school on <a href="/wiki/Avro_Canada" title="Avro Canada">Avro Road</a> in <a href="/wiki/Maple,_Ontario" title="Maple, Ontario">Maple, Ontario</a>, Canada, was also opened in 1994 as <i>L'école élémentaire catholique Le Petit Prince</i>. Its enrollment expanded from 30 students in its first year to some 325 children by 2014. One of Saint-Exupéry's colourful paintings of the prince is found on its website's welcome page.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CSDCCS_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-CSDCCS-157"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Little_Prince&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><b>Notes</b></p>
<div class="reflist references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Note that although Saint-Exupéry's regular French publisher, Gallimard, lists <i>Le Petit Prince</i> as being published in 1946, that is apparently a legalistic interpretation possibly designed to allow for an extra year of the novella's copyright protection period, and is based on Gallimard's explanation that the book was only 'sold' starting in 1946. Other sources, such as LePetitPrince.com,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LePetitPrince.net.b_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-LePetitPrince.net.b-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> record the first Librairie Gallimard printing of 12,250 copies as occurring on 30 November 1945.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The first English translation by Katherine Woods was published in the United States in April 1943, approximately one week prior to its first French printing by the same publisher, <a href="/wiki/Reynal_%26_Hitchcock" title="Reynal &amp; Hitchcock">Reynal &amp; Hitchcock</a>. Saint-Exupéry, a fiercely patriotic military pilot, had wisely fled occupied France after the German invasion of WWII and after interrogation by German authorities in Paris, and his literary works were subsequently banned by the <a href="/wiki/Vichy_France" title="Vichy France">Vichy government</a>. <i>Le Petit Prince</i> would not be published in France until after its liberation, with <a href="/wiki/%C3%89ditions_Gallimard" title="Éditions Gallimard">Gallimard</a>'s first printing in November 1945.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Antoine de Saint-Exupéry Foundation estimates an additional 80 million copies of the story in audio-video formats have been sold worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LittlePrince.com-a_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-LittlePrince.com-a-11"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although Saint-Exupéry was a master of the French language, he was never able to achieve anything more than haltingly poor English. Adèle Breaux, his young <a href="/wiki/Northport,_New_York" title="Northport, New York">Northport</a> English tutor to whom he later dedicated a writing ("For Miss Adèle Breaux, who so gently guided me in the mysteries of the English language.") related her experiences with her famous student as <i>Saint-Exupéry in America, 1942-1943: A Memoir</i>, published in 1971.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Saint-Exupéry's prodigious writings and studies of literature sometimes gripped him, and on occasion he continued his readings of literary works until moments before takeoff on solitary military reconnaissance flights, as he was adept at both reading and writing while flying. Taking off with an open book balanced on his leg, his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ground_crew" title="Ground crew">ground crew</a> would fear his mission would quickly end after contacting something "very hard". On one flight, to the chagrin of colleagues awaiting his arrival, he circled the Tunis airport for an hour so that he could finish reading a novel. Saint-Exupéry frequently flew with a lined <i>carnet</i> (notebook) during his long, solo flights, and some of his philosophical writings were created during such periods when he could reflect on the world below him, becoming "enmeshed in a search for ideals which he translated into fable and parable".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchiff2006_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchiff2006-27"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWebster1993251_.26_260_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWebster1993251_.26_260-28"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-32">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The plane Saint-Exupéry was flying when he crashed at high speed in the Sahara was a <a href="/wiki/Caudron" title="Caudron">Caudron</a> C-630 <a href="/wiki/Caudron_Simoun" title="Caudron Simoun">Simoun</a>, Serial Number 7042, with the French registration F-ANRY ('F' being the international designator for France, and the remainder chosen by the author to represent ANtoine de saint-exupéRY).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-43">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to Hoffman: "<a href="/wiki/Anne_Morrow_Lindbergh" title="Anne Morrow Lindbergh">Anne Morrow Lindbergh</a>'s fascination with Saint-Ex was transparent in all she wrote about him, as might be expected when one aviator-writer romantic is writing about another." Saint-Exupéry visited with Anne for two days but spoke with <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lindbergh" title="Charles Lindbergh">Charles Lindbergh</a>, who arrived home late, for an hour. Besides their vast differences on how <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Hitler</a> and the European conflict should be treated, Charles did not speak French, and Saint-Exupéry did not speak English. Their discussions, passed through Anne's meager French, were somewhat muted. But the excited conversation between Antoine and Anne soon blossomed "like monster flowers", with each finishing the other's sentences. Ironically, while Saint-Exupéry would later campaign for an early U.S. entry into the war, Lindbergh strongly opposed American involvement in the European war and would favour a peace treaty with Hitler, similar to Stalin's. The meeting between the two future P-38 war pilots was termed "less than a rousing success". Moreover, Charles later became unhappy about his wife's vast esteem for the French adventurer."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-58">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Another source states that it was co-publisher Curtice Hitchcock who viewed the author sketches and doodles at a supper party one evening, and then suggested writing a children's book to Saint-Exupéry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-1942.12.06_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1942.12.06-57"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> An additional likely reason for the publisher's encouragement: <a href="/wiki/P._L._Travers" title="P. L. Travers">P.L. Travers</a>, the author of the popular children's books series on <i><a href="/wiki/Mary_Poppins" title="Mary Poppins">Mary Poppins</a></i>, was at that time working on her third installment that would be published by a Reynal &amp; Hitchcock competitor in 1943, the same year as <i>The Little Prince</i>. Saint-Exupéry's U.S. publisher pressed him to have a competing children's book on the market for Christmas 1942.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-61"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-61">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Saint-Exupéry was 43 the year the fable was published, and 44 the year he died. He originally wrote the story with 43 sunsets, but posthumous editions often quote '44 sunsets', possibly in tribute.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-71"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-71">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">On one of Saint-Exupéry's flights his aircraft engine started failing. His aircraft mechanic onboard later recalled that Saint-Exupéry was completely nonplussed, "Saint-Ex simply started doodling cartoons which he handed back to me with a big grin."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-1993.10.28_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-1993.10.28-15"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-81"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-81">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Following one of his crashes in a sophisticated single-pilot spy aircraft that resulted in him being grounded, Saint-Exupéry spared no effort in his campaign to return to active combat flying duty. He utilized all his contacts and powers of persuasion to overcome his age and physical handicap barriers, which would have completely barred an ordinary patriot from serving as a war pilot. Instrumental in his reinstatement was an agreement he proposed to <a href="/wiki/John_Phillips_(photographer)" title="John Phillips (photographer)">John Phillips</a>, a fluently bilingual <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life_Magazine" title="Life Magazine">Life Magazine</a></i> correspondent in February 1944, where Saint-Exupéry committed to "...write, and I'll donate what I do to you, for your publication, if you get me reinstated into my squadron."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchiff2006421_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchiff2006421-80"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> Phillips later met with a high-level U.S. Army Air Forces press officer in Italy, Colonel John Reagan McCrary, who conveyed the <i>Life Magazine</i> request to General Eaker. Eaker's approval for Saint-Exupéry's return to flying status would be made "...not through favoritism, but through exception." The brutalized French, it was noted, would cut a German's throat "...probably with more relish than anybody."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-83"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-83">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Various sources state that his final flight was either his seventh, eight, ninth, or even his tenth covert reconnaissance mission. He volunteered for almost every such proposed mission submitted to his squadron, and protested fiercely after being grounded following his second sortie which ended with a demolished P-38. His connections in high places, plus a publishing agreement with <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life_Magazine" title="Life Magazine">Life Magazine</a></i>, were instrumental in having the grounding order against him lifted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eyheramonno_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eyheramonno-82"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> For some time Saint-Exupéry's friends, colleagues, and compatriots were actively working to keep the aging, accident-prone author grounded, out of harm's way.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-100"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-100">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 2009, the director of the <i>Village Petite France</i> (Little France Village) in South Korea stated that there were 350 different editions of <i>Orin Wanja</i> (The Little Prince) in Korean, including editions in Manga.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SaintExup.C3.A9ry.com-a_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-SaintExup.C3.A9ry.com-a-99"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-105"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-105">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A further complication occurred due to Saint-Exupéry's opinions of French General <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle" title="Charles de Gaulle">Charles de Gaulle</a>, whom he held in low regard. Even though both men were working to free France from Nazi occupation, Saint-Exupéry saw de Gaulle with apprehension and consequently provided no public support to the General. In response, de Gaulle struck back at the author by implying that the author was a German supporter, and then had all his literary works banned in <a href="/wiki/Resistance_during_World_War_II" title="Resistance during World War II">France's North African colonies</a>. Saint-Exupéry's writings were, with irony, banned simultaneously in both <a href="/wiki/German_military_administration_in_occupied_France_during_World_War_II" title="German military administration in occupied France during World War II">occupied France</a> and <a href="/wiki/Free_France" title="Free France">Free France</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchiff1996414_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchiff1996414-104"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2000.06.25_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2000.06.25-78"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-124"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-124">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although <a href="/wiki/Macaulay_Culkin" title="Macaulay Culkin">Macaulay Culkin</a> had been earning approximately $8 million per film project at that point, he provided his narration to the museum "...for nothing, and we are grateful for his services", according to a Morgan representative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garden_City_Telegram-1994.09.29_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Garden_City_Telegram-1994.09.29-123"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-129"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-129">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <i>d'Gay</i> portion of the estate refers to Saint-Exupéry's married sister.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-132"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-132">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Orson_Welles" title="Orson Welles">Orson Welles</a> purchased the movie rights to the story the day after reading the novella in a single sitting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fine_Books_Magazine-2013.12.03_64-5"><a href="#cite_note-Fine_Books_Magazine-2013.12.03-64"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> Welles was unable to persuade <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney" title="Walt Disney">Walt Disney</a> to assist him in turning his screenplay of the story into a film, with Disney fearing such a screen release would upstage his own screen adaptations of other stories.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-133"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-133">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The signed copy is inscribed "For Stephen, to whom I have already spoken about the <i>The Little Prince</i>, and who perhaps will be his friend."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fast_Company_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fast_Company-60"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>
